Health Website Woes Widen As Insurers Get Wrong Data

By Christopher Weaver and Louise Radnofsky 
1,072 words
18 October 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Insurers say the federal health-care marketplace is generating flawed data that is straining their ability to handle even the trickle of enrollees who have gotten through so far, in a sign that technological problems extend further than the website traffic and software issues already identified.

Emerging errors include duplicate enrollments, spouses reported as children, missing data fields and suspect eligibility determinations, say executives at more than a dozen health plans. Blue Cross & Blue Shield of Nebraska said it had to hire temporary workers to contact new customers directly to resolve inaccuracies in submissions. Medical Mutual of Ohio said one customer had successfully signed up for three of its plans.

The flaws could do lasting damage to the law if customers are deterred from signing up or mistakenly believe they have obtained coverage.

"The longer this takes to resolve . . . the harder it will be to get people to [come back and] sign up," said Aetna Inc. Chief Executive Mark Bertolini. "It's not off to a great start," he said, though he believes the marketplaces are "here to stay."

The new troubles for the Affordable Care Act arrive as Washington's attention is expected to sharply shift toward scrutiny of the site's rocky rollout, whose problems had been overshadowed by a two-week brawl over the government shutdown and debt ceiling.

Pressure is rising on the Obama administration to fix the problems. A number of Republicans have urged Kathleen Sebelius, secretary of the Department of Health and Human Services, to resign. Prominent Democrats, including House Ways and Means ranking member Rep. Sander Levin (D., Mich.), have called for fixes to be accelerated. The White House has said it has full confidence in Ms. Sebelius.

HHS, which is running all or part of the marketplaces in 36 states, has repeatedly declined to answer specific questions about its handling of the rollout, including specific glitches, enrollment figures, or its plans to fix the problems.

"We know that people are enrolling in coverage and the system works. As individual problems are raised by insurers, we work aggressively to address them," HHS spokeswoman Joanne Peters said Thursday.

Health-department officials have pressured insurers to refrain from commenting publicly about the problems, according to executives at four health plans, who asked not to be named. The HHS declined to comment.

In prolonging the battles over the budget and debt ceiling, "all Republicans did was give [President Obama] great cover for the complete screw-up on the opening of the exchanges," said Gail Wilensky, a Medicare director in the administration of George H.W. Bush and UnitedHealth Group Inc. board member.

But the persistence of the technological problems could force a steeper political price for the Obama administration as Republican lawmakers redirect their focus. The GOP-led House Energy and Commerce Committee announced Thursday night it would hold a hearing next week on the rollout of the law and called on HHS to "voluntarily" make officials available after the secretary's staff said she couldn't come. Ms Peters said the department intended to be "responsive" to the request.

Under the law, most Americans would have to buy coverage starting next year, or pay a fine.

The latest round of problems has emerged after a technical bottleneck that blocked many potential customers from accessing the marketplace began to clear this week. People familiar with the development of the exchange said some technical problems improved this week.

Of 209,000 users who began to register on healthcare.gov on Monday or Tuesday of this week, just over one-quarter finished the process, according to an estimate made by the analytics firm comScore for The Wall Street Journal. In the first week, only 10% did so. The estimates are based on a sampling of Internet users tracked by the company.

As more of those users attempted to sign up for plans this week, insurers began noticing problems with enrollment data. For now, they say they are largely able to manually correct the errors. But as enrollment increases -- up to 7 million consumers are expected to sign up in the next 5 1/2 months -- that may not be possible, they worry.

Scott & White Health Plan in Temple, Texas, has received 25 enrollees from the federally run exchange so far. "There are some missing data elements that are requiring a lot of research on our part," said Allan Einboden, the health plan's chief executive. "If we'd received 5,000 and they all had to be worked, that's a lot of extra administrative costs," said Mr. Einboden, who said he expects the problems to be fixed.

After realizing that some applications listed up to three spouses in a single family, Blue Cross & Blue Shield of Nebraska, which has about 50 health-law enrollees, had to "stop those enrollments from going through the automated process," said Matt Leonard, the insurer's sales manager. "It takes an automated process and turns it into a manual process," he said.

At Priority Health in Michigan, health-plan staff are calling new customers to confirm each of their "couple of dozen" enrollees accurately picked the plan, said Joan Budden, chief marketing officer, after realizing some had enrolled in multiple health plans, likely owing to user error linked to slow healthcare.gov response times. "Sometimes they pushed the [submit] button three times," Ms. Budden said.

As consumers struggle to navigate healthcare.gov, some health-plan executives worry that only the sickest -- those who most expect to need insurance -- will persist in seeking coverage. If younger consumers who are on the fence about buying coverage find the process too onerous, insurers may end up with too few healthier members to offset the costs of less-healthy enrollees.

Tara Seidenberg, a 48-year-old paralegal from suburban Houston with multiple sclerosis, says she is likely to put up with all kinds of hurdles to buy coverage. After days of failed attempts to sign up on healthcare.gov, she is taking a break to wait for the glitches to resolve. She takes medications that cost $4,600 a month and her current coverage won't be available next year. "I'm pretty much guaranteed to try it again," Ms. Seidenberg said.

Special
Is Hedge-Fund Advertising Good or Bad for Investors?

1,591 words
16 October 2013
10:09
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Hedge funds can now advertise. But it remains to be seen what this will mean for individuals.

With this in mind, we asked The Experts: Is the lifting of the ban on hedge-fund advertising good or bad for investors?

This discussion relates to a recent Journal Report article on how to look under a hedge fund's hood and formed the basis of a discussion on The Experts blog on Oct. 8.

GEORGE PAPADOPOULOS: At first, I had mixed feelings about it. I can understand the reasons behind the Securities and Exchange Commission voting to change this rule that has been in place for decades. As part of the Jumpstart Our Business Startups Act (JOBS Act), this rule intended to make it easier for companies to raise funds, and presumably hire more people. Our economy can certainly use more jobs. We will just have to see how much of this happens in practice.

I do not expect to see commercials for hedge funds while watching CNN or the Super Bowl anytime soon. Remember, the hedge-fund industry has long been quite secretive and thrived on this exclusivity. Furthermore, it will be interesting to see how mutual funds react to this new entrant onto their turf and, most importantly, the variation that will undoubtedly develop in touting performance numbers. The mutual-fund industry must adhere to uniform rules on how to advertise performance; the hedge-fund industry does not. I can see a commercial already claiming, "The XYZ hedge fund beat all U.S.-based mutual funds by X% last month. Get in NOW!"

Investors must be extra vigilant, and they still are better off steering clear of hedge funds. Not only will they likely end up with more money in their pockets; they will certainly sleep better at night.

For a collection of hilarious tweets on this subject please check this article: "Hedge Fund Ads Allowed by SEC: Some Creative Slogans."

George Papadopoulos is a fee-only wealth manager in Novi, Mich., serving affluent individuals and families. You can follow him at twitter (@feeonlyplanner), connect with him at Google+ or visit his firm's website.

MANISHA THAKOR: Ironically, I think lifting the ban on hedge-fund advertising is good for investors. As someone who thinks it's the rare average individual who should even consider investing in a hedge fund, that may sound odd.

The reason I support advertising is that it lifts the veil of secrecy and exclusivity that has so long surrounded the hedge-fund world. Let's face it, it's just plain old human nature to want what we can't have. By peering behind the screen, the discerning potential individual investor can see for themselves the generally hefty fee structures and often surprisingly lackluster returns relative to overall market indexes. (Don't take my word for it, review the eye-popping data as compiled by my BAM Alliance colleague Larry Swedroe for yourself).

As Groucho Marx said, "I refuse to join any club that would have me as a member." Lifting the ban on advertising may encourage more individuals to say the same thing about hedge funds.

Manisha Thakor (@ManishaThakor) is the founder and CEO of Santa Fe, N.M.-based MoneyZen Wealth Management VLLC, an independent registered investment adviser and member of the BAM Alliance).

TOM BRAKKE: It is hard to see any benefit for investors, since those qualified (in a legal sense) to invest in hedge funds already could do so.

For perspective, look at the mutual-fund industry. It spends huge money on advertising and other marketing activities, and it doesn't seem that investors have gotten more educated about investing as a result or received very good investment outcomes overall. Many mutual-fund firms appear to spend a lot more money and energy on gathering assets than on managing them well.

Of late, lackluster performance by hedge funds has led to a debate about whether it makes sense to invest in them at all. There has been no shortage of big paydays for hedge-fund managers, however. No wonder that hedge funds have been called "a compensation scheme rather than an asset class." (For what it's worth, that saying has been around at least 20 years.)

As is true in every part of the investment business, there will always be some hedge funds that do better than others for a period of time. Just like with mutual funds, those stars often turn into shooting stars about the time the advertising campaigns really kick into gear.

Tom Brakke, CFA (@researchpuzzler) is a consultant, writer, and investment advisor who specializes in the analysis of investment decision making and the communication of investment ideas.

CHARLES ROTBLUT : The lifting of the ban on hedge-fund advertising is a win for the industry and a loss for investors. The advertising is going to lure in investors who don't understand what they are investing in.

The term "hedge fund" has become a catchall phrase for a vehicle that invests in any strategy a manager can create. Though there are probably some funds that still attempt to hedge against certain risks, many other funds focus either on a total return or some type of alternative return rather than truly attempting to hedge risk. Some of these strategies are very complex and not even easy for those of us who are professionals to grasp by simply reading a prospectus.

A recent survey by Charles Schwab supports my reason for concern. Over half of 401(k) participants surveyed said "401(k) investment info is more confusing than health-care benefits info." If investors don't understand their mutual-fund choices, they are certainly not going to understand their hedge-fund choices.

Then there is the issue of actual benefit. Hedge funds follow a "heads we win, tails you lose" type of fee system. The traditional fee is 2% of assets managed and 20% of profits realized. Making matters worse, the HFRX Global Hedge Fund Index lost 13.6% over the past five years, according to Barry Ritholtz of Fusion IQ.

Finally, there is the question of why a top-performing hedge fund would advertise at all. Too much in assets under management ruins returns, as has occurred in the mutual-fund space. Thus, investors would be wise to follow a paraphrased version of Groucho Marx's observation and refuse to join any hedge fund that would have them as a shareholder.

Charles Rotblut (@charlesrotblut) is a vice president with the American Association of Individual Investors .

RICK FERRI: The only item that has changed is that hedge funds are now allowed to advertise like mutual funds. That may make some hedge funds more visible, but I'm not sure it's in the best interest of people to buy these funds. Aren't the best funds supposed to be exclusive offerings that only the chosen few are invited to buy into? I see a potential red flag in hedge funds that need to reach out to the general public for funding.

I don't invest in hedge funds or recommend them to my clients. Why make things more complicated than they need to be? A portfolio of low-cost index funds is all that's needed for financial success.

Rick Ferri is founder of Portfolio Solutions LLC and the author of six books on low-cost index fund and ETF investing. His blog is RickFerri.com.

MATT HOUGAN: That strikes me as the wrong question. We've let mutual funds that charge way too much, offer terrible transparency and deliver subpar returns advertise direct to consumers for decades. Why should hedge funds be any different?

Given that past performance is no guarantee of future returns, the bigger advertising question we should ask is: Should any fund company be allowed to advertise returns of any kind to investors? My guess is we'd be a lot better off if they couldn't.

Matt Hougan (@Matt_Hougan) is president of ETF analytics and global head of editorial for IndexUniverse LLC.

JOHN ROGERS : So long as the advertising adheres to common and accepted standards of performance presentation, such as the Global Investment Performance Standards, then this has the potential for benefiting investors.

CFA Institute has long held that relevant, timely and complete information is necessary for investors to make informed decisions, and advertising in this manner may assist investors in that goal. The key to positive results, however, is for regulators to ensure that the advertising is directed at so-called accredited investors who have the means, experience and understanding sufficient to help them discern fact from flattering exaggeration.

John Rogers is the president and chief executive of the CFA Institute.

LARRY ZIMPLEMAN : It will be interesting to see how the lifting of the ban on hedge-fund advertising may cause other changes in the traditional hedge-fund industry.

Historically, hedge funds have restricted their activity to institutional investors and to individuals who have substantial net worth thus, not a lot of need for general retail advertising. Now that the ban is lifted, will some hedge funds seek to find ways to offer products that might appeal to more traditional retail investors? I think that any changes will occur relatively slowly and over time. But, in the long run, it may open up some additional investing options for the more typical retail investor.

Larry D. Zimpleman is chairman, president and chief executive of Principal Financial Group .

Star Manager Neil Woodford to Leave Invesco; Plans New Business Serving Institutional, Retail Clients

Joe McGrath 
495 words
16 October 2013
04:11
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Invesco Perpetual's multibillion-dollar man is quitting to set up his own fund.

Neil Woodford, who personally manages about 27 billion ($43 billion) across five separate funds, said in a statement that he is planning to set up a new fund-management business aimed at institutional and retail clients next year.

Mr. Woodford, who has been at Invesco for 25 years, has become one of the U.K.'s highest-profile fund managers. He was made a Commander of the Order of the British Empire this year for services to the economy. His departure will likely be seen as a blow for the Atlanta-based investment firm.

Its U.K. arm, Invesco Perpetual, is one of Britain's largest money managers.

"Clearly, there are a number of very good portfolio managers at Invesco Perpetual but Neil Woodford has been the name behind their strategy and success in recent years," said Edward Rogers, senior investment manager at JM Finn, a British investment-management firm.

The departure of a high-profile manager can trigger outflows. This year, U.K. equities manager Richard Buxton left Schroders as head of the Schroder UK Alpha Plus fund in March. Around 1.7 billion left the Schroders fund between the end of March and early August after Mr. Buxton's move to rival Old Mutual.

Liontrust Asset Management suffered a drop in assets under management of more than 2 billion following the departure of two star managers in January 2009. Before the exit of Jeremy Lang and Bill Pattisson, who ran about 90% of Liontrust's funds between them, assets under management stood at 3.4 billion. By March of 2009, assets had fallen to 1.1 billion.

"I expect many Woodford followers will migrate with him in due course," said Jeremy Batstone-Carr, chief economist and strategist at London-based brokerage Charles Stanley.

"Most of Woodford's money, totally over 30 billion, is invested with him because of who he is and his success," Tim Cockerill, investment director at Rowan Dartington, said.

Some of the funds Mr. Woodford manages are among the strongest performers in the industry. The 13.9 billion Invesco Perpetual High Income had annualized returns of 12.2% over the 10 years ending September 2013, while the 10.6 billion income fund has achieved 12.1% over the same period, according to FE Analytics. The benchmark return of the Investment Management Association Equity Income sector was 8.5% over the same period.

Mr. Woodford is to continue managing both funds until he leaves Invesco on April 29, when Mark Barnett will take over as head of U.K. equities. Mr. Woodford couldn't be reached to comment.

Mark Armour, chief executive of Invesco Perpetual, said succession has been planned for many years. Mr. Woodford started out at Dominion Insurance in 1981 before joining insurer Eagle Star six years later.

Invesco Perpetual's multibillion-dollar man, Neil Woodford, will leave the fund manager, one of the U.K.'s largest, next April, with the aim of setting up a new fund-management business.

Mr. Woodford will stay on as manager of the GBP 13.9 billion ($22.2 billion) Invesco Perpetual High Income and GBP 10.6 billion Income funds until his departure on April 29, 2014, when Mark Barnett will take over as head of U.K. equities.

"My intention is to establish a new fund-management business serving institutional and retail clients as soon as possible after 29th April 2014. There will be no further comment at this stage."

The departure of a high-profile manager can trigger outflows. This year, U.K. equities manager Richard Buxton left Schroders as head of the Schroder UK Alpha Plus fund in March. Prior to the exit of Jeremy Lang and Bill Pattisson, who ran about 90% of Liontrust's funds between them, assets under management stood at GBP 3.4 billion. By March of 2009, assets had fallen to GBP 1.1 billion.

"I expect many Woodford followers will migrate with him in due course," said Jeremy Batstone-Carr, chief economist and strategist at London-based brokerage Charles Stanley.

Mr. Woodford started out at Dominion Insurance in 1981 before joining Eagle Star six years later. He is a graduate of Exeter University and London Business School.

Mark Armour, chief executive of Invesco Perpetual, acknowledged Mr. Woodford's considerable contribution to the company over the past 25 years and said he was ready for new challenges.

Mr. Armour said the succession has been in the planning for many years and that Mr. Barnett was an "exceptional" fund manager.

Ciaran Mallon is to take responsibility for the U.K. equity part of the Monthly Income Plus and Distribution funds, effective immediately, working alongside existing fixed-income managers Paul Causer and Paul Read. Mr. Woodford previously ran the U.K. equity element of these funds.

Personal Finance
A J.P. Morgan Doctorate?; The Bank May Need One to Understand Itself

Al Lewis 
606 words
13 October 2013
01:01
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

J.P. Morgan Chase has grown so complex that people now have to get doctorate degrees in J.P. Morgan Chase.

America's biggest bank by assets is trying to buy itself a doctoral program at the University of Delaware. It has offered to spend $17 million to help students earn advanced degrees in "financial analytics," according to a report last week by Insider Higher Ed.

Some faculty are calling it the "J.P. Morgan Ph.D." They argue the program would cross a line between financial support and financial control of a quasi-public university. The $2.5 trillion bank wants to select the faculty that would run the program and even place its own employees on dissertation committees, roles traditionally left to academicians.

Professors also complain that the bank's grip on the school is tightening with each donation. It has even set up an office on campus using student interns. "I like banks and corporations but not on campus," Sheldon Pollack, a law and political-science professor told The Atlantic.

If this were a master's program, an arrangement like this would be acceptable, Mr. Pollack explained. M.B.A.s, after all, are professional degrees. But this is a doctoral program, and doctorates are academic degrees.

Such purist views are almost always sidelined by money. The fact is, J.P. Morgan has grown so large it needs Ph.D.s to help it understand itself. Course work could include a host of topics that have emerged in just the past few years:

"How to trade like the London Whale." Explain how the bank lost $6 billion in reckless derivatives trades and had to pay regulators a $920 million fine for "unsafe and unsound practices."

"How to properly foreclose on a home." Explain why the bank had to pony up billions for wrongfully foreclosing on active-duty military personnel as well as what regulators called "shoddy loan servicing, illegal robo-signing, and faulty foreclosure processing."

"How to bounce checks." Explain why J.P. Morgan had to settle litigation alleging it processed its customers' checks by size, rather than the order in which they were cashed, so it could bag unwarranted overdraft fees.

"Define 'misappropriation of funds.' " Explain why J.P. Morgan was forced to repay $546 million to MF Global customers after its collapse.

"How to manipulate energy prices." Explain why J.P. Morgan had to pay $410 million in fines for allegedly engaging in the same kind of illegal activity as Enron.

"How to rig the bond market." Explore J.P. Morgan's $229 million settlement with federal and state authorities for allegedly gaming the bidding process for bond deals affecting 31 states.

"What is a 'fortress balance sheet'?" J.P. Morgan CEO James Dimon used to brag that his bank had a "fortress balance sheet." It didn't need the bailout it got during the 2008 financial crisis, but was forced to take it, he said. Investigate whether this is true.

Complexity is hitting the bottom line: On Friday the bank reported its first quarterly loss under Mr. Dimon $380 million, compared with a year-earlier $5.7 billion profit.

I think J.P. Morgan gets in trouble not because Mr. Dimon is a bad guy, but because its sheer size makes it unmanageable. It has thousands of Type-A personalities running over themselves to get deals done. No one can keep a firm handle on them all.

Candidates for a J.P. Morgan Ph.D. will need all the help they can get.

U.S. News: Health Exchange's Flaws Pinpointed

By Christopher Weaver and Louise Radnofsky 
837 words
11 October 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
A4
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Government officials are considering rebuilding some parts of the federally run health-insurance marketplace that have been identified as the key flaws that blocked many consumers from getting coverage.

Much of the problem stems from a design element that requires users of the federal site, which serves 36 states, to create accounts before shopping for insurance, according to policy and technology experts. The site, healthcare.gov, was initially going to include an option to browse before registering, but that tool was delayed, people familiar with the situation said.

The decision to move ahead without that feature proved crucial because, before users can begin shopping for coverage, they must cross a busy digital junction in which data are swapped among computer systems built or run by contractors including CGI Group Inc., the healthcare.gov developer; Quality Software Services Inc., a UnitedHealth Group Inc. unit; and credit-checker Experian PLC.

If any part of the web of systems fails to work properly, it could lead to a traffic jam blocking most users from the marketplace. That's just what happened: On Oct. 2, officials identified a bottleneck where those systems intersect at a software component sold by Oracle Corp. that still hasn't been cleared.

The series of decisions and technical stumbles came together like a perfect storm amid political pressure to open the marketplaces on the health-overhaul law's deadline of Oct. 1, according to the accounts of multiple people involved in the exchange and experts following the developments. As a result, many fewer people are able to navigate the marketplace than called for in the initial plan, said people familiar with the situation.

Former officials at the Department of Health and Human Services say the decision to require account creation came as a surprise to them and were critical of it. "People should be able to get [insurance] quotes" without going through the technical hurdles upfront, said Jay Angoff, a former director of the federal office overseeing the development of the marketplace.

The problems haven't yet been resolved, but people familiar with the situation said officials are debating whether to replace parts of the registration system this weekend. By Thursday morning, a new tool that allows users to preview plans without registering appeared on the site with little fanfare.

The work has led to improvements in recent days, people familiar with the matter said. HHS officials said some consumers were able to complete applications, though they have declined to specify how many people.

Of just over two million users who visited healthcare.gov's registration page through last Sunday, about 125,000 made it to the end of the account creation process, according to an estimate by Internet analytics company comScore. The estimate, made at The Wall Street Journal's request, was based on a sampling of computer users tracked by the company.

An HHS spokeswoman, Joanne Peters, declined to answer detailed questions provided Wednesday and Thursday by the Journal. The department said government information-technology experts and contractors were working around the clock to improve the website's performance. "We have a plan in place and are making progress," Ms. Peters said Wednesday.

The main bottleneck federal officials are now plumbing occurs as the slate of registration systems intersect with Oracle Identity Manager, a software component embedded in a government identity-checking system, according to people familiar with the situation. Late on Wednesday, Oct. 2, Oracle sent a handful of experts from California, where the firm is based, to the Washington area to begin work on the problems, some of the people said.

"Our software is the identical product deployed in most of the world's most complex systems" and is working correctly, Oracle said in a statement issued by Deborah Hellinger, vice president of corporate communications. Oracle engineers have worked with other contractors to fix configurations and add server capacity, she said.

Obama administration officials initially attributed the site's problems to an unexpectedly high volume of Web traffic driven by consumer enthusiasm for coverage under the law -- some nine million visitors as of last Friday. The administration has conceded that software and design issues also contributed to hang-ups.

Outside technology experts and people close to the situation say the first week and half of exchange operations has revealed a half-finished product.

"The fatal mistake they made is bringing up everything at once," making debugging the sprawling system a challenge, said David Starr, a former chief information officer for 3Com Corp., ITT Corp. and other large companies.

Federal officials had debated internally whether to allow users to view plans without applying, according to people familiar with the process.

An HHS spokeswoman said the agency wanted to ensure that users were aware of their eligibility for subsidies that could help pay for coverage, before they started seeing the prices of policies.

Business
What CFOs Are Saying About the Shutdown; Companies' Businesses Could Suffer Because of Furloughs and Agency Closures

By Maxwell Murphy and James Willhite 
969 words
10 October 2013
20:56
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

As companies begin reporting third-quarter financial results this week, executives are warning that a long-term government shutdown could crimp their businesses, especially if they are close to federal installations or have customers near the poverty line.

Scores of companies in nearly 200 public filings have already cautioned investors that their businesses could suffer from the shutdown, in its ninth day Wednesday. Among them: J.C. Penney Co ., Family Dollar Stores Inc . and Humana Inc ., which all warned this week of the potential bottom-line consequences of broad federal furloughs and agency closures.

"Over half of our customers are on &some sort of government assistance," Howard Levine , chief executive of Family Dollar Stores, told investors Wednesday. The current and potential economic effects of the shutdown "are very real to our customer every day."

In addition, corporate plans for mergers and acquisitions could be put on hold as executives wait for the government to reach an agreement on a new budget and a higher debt ceiling.

Mergers typically require antitrust and government reviews, said Keith Moore , a managing director with MKM Partners LLC , an equity research firm, and though various agencies are accepting applications, it remains uncertain when they will be able to provide clearances. Plus, the risk of a U.S. government default on its debt could roil stock markets and make any negotiated deal premiums look foolish in retrospect.

"Over the past two weeks we saw what seemed to be an acceleration" in deal making, Mr. Moore said. "But there was nothing on Monday," which is often the busiest day of the week.

Every week that the government is closed trims economic output by an estimated $800 million, or 0.08 percentage point for the quarter, according to the economic research firm IHS Inc . "If the shutdown drags on," it said, "the effects will start to add up."

The biggest casualties will be government contractors, aerospace and defense companies and firms, such as pharmaceutical makers, that rely on agencies such as the Food and Drug Administration for approvals. But in addition, companies that serve budget-conscious customers, such as Costco Wholesale Corp ., are feeling the impact.

When the government last shut down for 21 days ending in January 1996, the gross domestic product was sliced by 0.5 percentage point for the 1995 fourth quarter, and "the technology boom of that time made underlying growth much stronger than it is today," said Mark Zandi, chief economist at Moody's Analytics.

Intel Corp . Chief Financial Officer Stacy Smith is worried about another battle over raising the debt ceiling.

"We saw it with the last fight: It had a real impact on the economy for the past couple of quarters," he said in an interview last month. "It impacted people's confidence."

And with the year-end shopping season approaching, that is critical, especially for retailers. According to a transcript from S&P Capital IQ, Charles Bergh , chief executive of Levi Strauss & Co ., told investors last week, "If that is a prolonged [shutdown], that is going to have a market impact, I think, on everyone's business as we cruise into the holiday season."

Stocks had been stoic toward the beginning of the shutdown, but by Wednesday's close the Dow Jones Industrial Average was down more than 2%.

In addition, the price to borrow cash against securities such as Treasurys is rising, and some lenders are unwilling to accept Treasury securities maturing in October as backing for their loans. The Treasury Department has indicated it could reach its borrowing capacity around Oct. 17.

Penney on Tuesday added the following disclaimer to its list of what could throw off its business projections: "the ability of the federal government to fund and conduct its operations."

A Penney spokeswoman said the company's "risk factors encompass everything that &could materially adversely affect our business [including] factors beyond our control."

Wolverine World Wide Inc ., meanwhile, was more specific. Its new risk-factor disclosure cautioned of "delays in importing products at U.S. ports, supply-chain disruption and reduced purchasing by the Department of Defense or other military purchasers."

A spokesman for the footwear maker didn't respond to a request for comment.

Humana said the Defense Health Agency told it last week that the agency can't make payments "until it has received a signed appropriation, continuing resolution or other legal authority." The agency also told Humana that, once it has that, "all payments due will be accelerated with interest."

The agency authorized Humana to continue to administer benefits for two weeks without payments, the company said. Humana didn't say what would happen after that, but it expects the claims submitted during that time will total about $175 million and doesn't believe the payment halt will have a material negative effect on its operations.

A Humana spokesman didn't respond to a request for additional comment.

On Wednesday, Costco reported a scant increase in sales growth. And despite the shaky economy, which has encouraged shoppers to make bulk purchases to save money, the wholesaler is already noticing a drop in customers around Washington, D.C., where many furloughed government employees live.

Asked by a stock analyst whether there was anything strategic Costco was doing because of the shutdown, CFO Richard Galanti deadpanned: "Other than scratching our head in disbelief?"

Lenders Scour Emails Amid Currency Probe

By Katie Martin, Chiara Albanese and Clare Connaghan 
911 words
10 October 2013
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
1
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Several of the world's largest banks have searched through emails and other electronic communications of employees in response to probes by European regulators into suspected manipulation of foreign-exchange markets, according to people familiar with the matter.

Royal Bank of Scotland Group PLC has turned over electronic communications by a former employee to Britain's Financial Conduct Authority, one person said. As part of the FCA investigation, which began in June, the largest banks in the business are examining their own trading patterns or communications -- including Deutsche Bank AG, Citigroup Inc. and Barclays PLC, according to other people familiar with the matter.

The actions indicate that ongoing examinations of currency markets by regulators in Switzerland, the U.K. and Brussels are gaining traction.

Early Wednesday, Swiss Finance Minister Eveline Widmer-Schlumpf raised eyebrows by telling reporters that manipulation of foreign-exchange rates "has taken place." Later in the day, however, a spokesman for the Swiss Federal Department of Finance said that she had misspoken and meant to say the Swiss market regulator was examining the issue. Switzerland's main market regulator, Finma, said Friday it was investigating several Swiss financial institutions for possible manipulation of exchange rates.

Much of the concern centers on "fixes" -- daily snapshots of where currencies are trading.

Some investors are already steering clear of these fixes. They worry that they are receiving disadvantageous prices for currency transactions, and that the process for determining the so-called fixes is too opaque.

Currencies trade around the globe and around the clock, so there is no moment when the market closes to use as a daily benchmark. Instead, data providers take periodic snapshots; the most popular is the price computed at 4 p.m. London time by WM/Reuters, a joint venture between State Street's WM unit and Thomson Reuters. That fix serves as a reference point, and many investors will ask their banks to process transactions at the fix price.

But some market participants have grown wary of the fix, which they say is plagued by unexplained price spikes and variable liquidity.

A spokeswoman for WM referred a reporter to a document on the company's website that describes the methodology for computing the fixes. A spokesman for Thomson Reuters said the company provides data from which WM calculates the benchmark. Thomson Reuters would cooperate with any regulators' enquiries, he said.

"Unless you are forced to trade at a certain benchmark, you are better off avoiding the fix," said Klaus Paesler, head of currency and overlay strategy for Europe, the Middle East and Africa at Russell Investments, a big asset manager that offers its own foreign-exchange services. "Fixes can be points of low liquidity and increased volatility with higher transaction costs versus the average price of the day."

In addition to the Swiss regulator, the EU's antitrust commission is looking at allegations of exchange-rate manipulation "at a very preliminary stage," said spokesman Antoine Colombani on Tuesday.

It wasn't clear when RBS turned over the electronic communications to the FCA, or whether those communications formed the bank's entire submission to the regulator. News that it had done so was earlier reported by Bloomberg.

The person familiar with the matter said the former employee left the bank some time ago for reasons unrelated to the probe.

The precise focus of the investigations also isn't clear.

The WM/Reuters fix is computed by examining trade data received from multiple execution venues over a period of 60 seconds around 4 p.m.

Major currency pairs, such as the euro-dollar or the pound-dollar, are heavily traded, which suggests collusion to influence the rate would be a difficult undertaking.

For less-liquid currencies, WM/Reuters uses bid and offer rates seen in the market and applies "further quality checks," according to the document posted on the WM website.

Fixes are especially popular with index funds designed to track the broader market. By executing currency trades at the fix price, funds can ensure that foreign-exchange fluctuations don't cause their returns to drift from the indexes.

But some investors and investment advisers believe the opaque nature of the calculations -- it's impossible to know whose big trades might be swinging the fix price up or down -- is a danger sign.

"Our clients, such as local pension funds, come to us with the explicit request to trade at fixes also because this is recommended by their investment consultants. But we have serious concerns over their use," said the head of trading at an asset-management firm. "They do not always guarantee the most transparent rate."

Stuart Catt of Mercer Consulting, which advises clients about investment strategy, said there were better alternatives to fixes.

"You can be less confident the price you get at 4 p.m. is good, as it's more likely to be affected by market activity," Mr. Catt said. Banks "are certainly aware of the volume of institutional client activity predicated on the 4 p.m. fix and seek to position their books to benefit from this."

James Cochrane, a director at ITG Analytics, said he didn't recommend the fix to his clients. "For one thing, the pricing is not always great, and for another, there is no one watching this, no watchdog," he said. "It is an unregulated benchmark."

Healthcare.Gov's Flaws Found, Fixes Eyed; Site's Design Opened Door to Bottleneck That Persists

By Christopher Weaver and Louise Radnofsky 
1,162 words
11 October 2013
00:24
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Government officials are considering rebuilding some parts of the federally run health-insurance marketplace that have been identified as the key flaws that blocked many consumers from getting coverage.

Much of the problem stems from a design element that requires users of the federal site, which serves 36 states, to create accounts before shopping for insurance, according to policy and technology experts. The site, healthcare.gov, was initially going to include an option to browse before registering, but that tool was delayed, people familiar with the situation said.

The decision to move ahead without that feature proved crucial because, before users can begin shopping for coverage, they must cross a busy digital junction in which data are swapped among separate computer systems built or run by contractors including CGI Group Inc., the healthcare.gov developer; Quality Software Services Inc., a UnitedHealth Group Inc. unit; and credit-checker Experian PLC.

If any part of the web of systems fails to work properly, it could lead to a traffic jam blocking most users from the marketplace. That's just what happened: On Oct. 2, officials identified a bottleneck where those systems intersect at a software component sold by Oracle Corp. that still hasn't been cleared.

The series of decisions and technical stumbles came together like a perfect storm amid political pressure to open the marketplaces on the health-overhaul law's deadline of Oct. 1, according to the accounts of multiple people involved in the exchange and experts following the developments. As a result, many fewer people are able to navigate the marketplace than called for in the initial plan, said people familiar with the situation.

Former officials at the Department of Health and Human Services say the decision to require account creation came as a surprise to them and were critical of it. "People should be able to get [insurance] quotes" without going through the technical hurdles upfront, said Jay Angoff, a former director of the federal office overseeing the development of the marketplace, also known as an exchange.

The problems haven't yet been resolved, but people familiar with the situation said officials are debating whether to replace parts of the registration system this weekend. By Thursday morning, a new tool that allows users to preview plans without registering appeared on the site with little fanfare.

The work has led to improvements in recent days, people familiar with the matter said. HHS officials said some consumers were able to complete applications, though they have declined to specify how many people.

Of just over two million users who visited healthcare.gov's registration page through last Sunday, about 125,000 made it to the end of the account creation process, according to an estimate by Internet analytics company comScore. The estimate, made at The Wall Street Journal's request, was based on a sampling of computer users tracked by the company.

An HHS spokeswoman, Joanne Peters, declined to answer detailed questions provided Wednesday and Thursday by the Journal. The department said government information-technology experts and contractors were working around the clock to improve the website's performance. "We have a plan in place and are making progress," Ms. Peters said Wednesday.

The main bottleneck federal officials are now plumbing occurs as the slate of registration systems intersect with Oracle Identity Manager, a software component embedded in a government identity-checking system, according to people familiar with the situation. Late on Wednesday, Oct. 2, Oracle sent a handful of experts from California, where the firm is based, to the Washington area to begin work on the problems, some of the people said.

Healthcare.gov's main application, developed by CGI, collects information from users as they begin creating accounts. It transfers that data to a Quality Software Services system used by the federal Medicare agency called Enterprise Identification Management. That system, in turn, submits data to Experian, which loads a series of questions used to confirm new users' identities.

Quality Software Services subcontracted with Oracle for the identity management system's key software component, according to people familiar with the matter. The other systems are not interfacing consistently with the Oracle software, blocking many users from completing the registration process, other people said.

"Our software is the identical product deployed in most of the world's most complex systems" and is working correctly, Oracle said in a statement issued by Deborah Hellinger, vice president of corporate communications. Oracle engineers have worked with other contractors to fix configurations and add server capacity, she said.

Obama administration officials initially attributed the site's problems to an unexpectedly high volume of Web traffic driven by consumer enthusiasm for coverage under the law some nine million visitors as of last Friday. The administration has conceded that software and design issues also contributed to hang-ups.

But officials maintained this week in conversations with congressional Democrats that the main issue with the site is its popularity and focused on expanded server capacity as a solution to that problem, congressional aides said.

Outside technology experts and people close to the situation say the first week and half of exchange operations has revealed a half-finished product.

"The fatal mistake they made is bringing up everything at once," making debugging the sprawling system a challenge, said David Starr, a former chief information officer for 3Com Corp., ITT Corp. and other large companies. But, "it's almost always better to postpone things than bring them up broken," said Mr. Starr, who is not involved in the exchange development.

The problem might have been largely averted if consumers had been able to browse plans without going through the complex registration process first, experts say.

"If you put a critical pathway at the front of the experience, you're going to logjam everything," said Patrick Byrne, chief executive of Overstock.com, an e-commerce company. "If you have a bunch of Web services talking to each other, you want to put the transaction at the end," he said.

Several states that took that approach 14 states are running their own exchanges have seen more success managing the tide of Web traffic. Washington state, California, Kentucky and other states that allow so-called window shopping have largely overcome early glitches. In Washington, for instance, 10,000 people have enrolled in coverage and thousands more have begun applying, officials said.

Federal officials had debated internally whether to allow users to view plans without applying, according to people familiar with the process.

An HHS spokeswoman said the agency wanted to ensure that users were aware of their eligibility for subsidies that could help pay for coverage, before they started seeing the prices of policies.

Shira Ovide and Joel Schectman contributed to this article.

COMPANIES
Arrow Global shares fly high on debut; General Financial

By Sharlene Goff 
419 words
9 October 2013
Financial Times
FTFTA
London Ed1
20
English
Copyright 2013 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Distressed debt group raises 50m

Sale of stake values business at 360m

Shares in Arrow Global, one of the UK's biggest debt recovery companies, jumped yesterday after the group made its debut on the stock market.

Arrow, which buys distressed consumer debt from banks and credit card companies, priced the sale of a 52.8 per cent stake at 205p, valuing the company at about 360m. The shares rose to a high of 227p before falling to 218.5p at the close.

The listing raised about 50m for the company and about 140m for its shareholders, which include a fund run by Royal Bank of Scotland . The RBS Special Opportunities Fund has retained slightly less than 30 per cent of Arrow's shares. Sir George Mathewson, former chief executive of RBS, is a non-executive director.

Arrow owns and manages about 8bn of distressed consumer debt and has about 5m customers. It buys the debt at deeply discounted prices - typically offering about 10p for every 1 - and attempts to squeeze out the value by reconnecting with borrowers. It expects to use the 50m it raised from the listing to purchase more packages of debt.

It is one of a number of debt management companies that have benefited from a buoyant market in recent months, as banks move to clean up their balance sheets and release capital by shedding soured debt. Rivals in the sector have also attracted investors - JC Flowers, the US private equity group, acquired Cabot Credit Management, another debt collector, from Anacap in a deal worth about 500m in May, for example.

Revenue at Arrow increased more than 50 per cent in the first six months of 2013, compared with a year earlier. Much of its work involves using technology and data analytics to identify and track down debtors after their contact information has been lost by the original lender. Once it has the information it uses third-party collectors to recoup the debt.

Tom Drury, chief executive of Arrow, said: "Our focus now is on continuing to drive growth, leveraging our sophisticated data and analytics capabilities, in order to offer investors a highly cash-generative business with significant earnings visibility.

"We also look forward to continuing our commitment to providing our customers with an excellent service based on a fair and collaborative approach."

Goldman Sachs acted as global co-ordinator on the listing and, along with Jefferies, was joint bookrunner.

Corporate News: Data Mining Evolves, Thanks to Twitter --- 'Social Listening Firms' Emerge, Analyzing the Site's Stream of Information for Insights on News and Trends

By Elizabeth Dwoskin 
1,258 words
8 October 2013
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
22
English
(c) 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns

In its IPO filing Thursday, Twitter Inc. disclosed how much the microblogging platform earned from a lesser-known side business: $47.5 million came from selling off its data to a fast-growing group of companies that analyze the data for insights into news events and trends.

That is a small amount compared with the revenue generated from advertising, but Twitter's data business has rippled across the economy. The site's constant stream of experiences, opinions and sentiments has spawned a vast commercial ecosystem, serving up putative insights to product developers, Hollywood studios, major retailers and -- potentially most profitably -- hedge funds and other investors. Backed by millions of dollars in venture capital, hundreds of "social listening" firms have emerged.

"The economic impact of Twitter is massively bigger than whatever lies in their [IPO] filing," says Rob Bailey, chief executive of Data Sift, a London-based social-data analytics company.

Social-data firms spot trends that it would take a long time for humans to see on their own. The United Nations is using algorithms derived from Twitter to pinpoint hot spots of social unrest. DirecTV uses Twitter data as an early-warning system to spot power outages based on customer complaints. Human-resources departments analyze the data to evaluate job candidates.

One opportunity may be on Wall Street. Dataminr Inc., which has raised $30 million in funding this year alone, has made serving traders its core business. It uses algorithms similar to those employed by hedge funds to conduct high-speed trading. Five minutes before news of last week's Capitol Hill shooting in Washington, D.C., broke on television, Dataminr subscribers got an alert to take action -- giving them a leg up on the news that led the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index to drop 20 points five minutes later.

Dataminr's algorithms spotted just a handful of early eyewitness shooting reports posted on Twitter and instantaneously deduced from their timing, along with the Tweeters' influence and physical location that something urgent was happening. Other trading algorithms use "sentiment scores" to track the mood about certain stocks, driving decisions about whether to be bullish or bearish on firms.

These social-data companies are subsets of a burgeoning data industry that collects, disseminates and analyzes all the information that firms amass and people leave online. While estimates of the market value of the social-data industry are hard to come by, one research firm, IDC, estimates that the entire "big data" market has grown seven times as quickly as the information-technology sector as a whole. It may be valued at $16.9 billion in two years.

Most of the social companies pull data from a range of sources, including Yahoo Inc.'s Tumblr, Facebook Inc.'s Instagram, and the review site Yelp Inc. But Twitter, because of its public real-time data stream, is the industry giant. Even the U.S. Library of Congress is now cataloging tweets.

Twitter's social-network rival Facebook, on the other hand, has largely eschewed getting involved in the data business. In part, that is because Facebook's product isn't set up for public conversations, but rather for private sharing among friends. But with an eye toward Twitter's success in leading public conversations about real-time events, Facebook recently has joined with major media companies to provide aggregated information about consumer conversations on certain topics.

Each social-data firm boasts proprietary dating-mining tools that go beyond basic keyword searches. Some can zoom in on a subset of people -- say, women in a certain postal zone -- and monitor phrases that show emotion. Then they can create a heat map or a sentiment score that measures how that subset feels about a topic. They have trained natural language processing algorithms to look at slang and broken grammar and to highlight tweets that indicate urgency because of words like "BREAKING."

"We don't just count the volume of these trends. That's naive," says Nova Spivack, chief executive of the Los Angeles-based firm Bottlenose. Rather, his firm looks at the momentum of trends.

"Businesses have spent years understanding the past," says Mr. Spivack. "Twitter has found a way to measure the present."

Twitter data aren't a perfect crystal ball. The service's most active U.S. users aren't entirely representative of the American population. According to the Pew Internet Project, though three-quarters of American adults are sharing information on social-networking sites, Twitter users, in particular, skew young -- 30% are under the age of 30. College graduates and wealthier Americans also have higher rates of Twitter use, but that gap is small, and narrowing.

Companies say it is still too early to determine the long-term value of Twitter data. One risk that Twitter raised in its IPO prospectus is that the company's growth could be undermined by spammers and fake Twitter accounts. Another risk: Twitter accounts can be hacked and used to put out incorrect information.

A Twitter spokesman declined to comment.

Companies are still struggling to understand how the Twitter conversation relates to the real world. Most aren't yet abandoning focus groups or customer-satisfaction surveys for social-media monitoring, says Darrell Jursa, of Fleishman-Hillard, a public-relations firm specializing in crisis management. Some "80% to 90% of conversations still take place offline," he says. "You need to put the social data in context with everything else."

Moreover, companies that tap the data are increasingly trying to figure out whether a Twitter "crisis" actually resonates with all Americans or stays in an echo chamber.

Restaurant-chain Five Guys used Washington, D.C.-based New Brand Analytics to compare hamburger-bun quality in different parts of the country, leading the company to flag issues in some areas. But the firm warned the chain not to overreact to complaints, says Five Guys spokeswoman Molly Catalano. While some individual complaints were nasty, overall Five Guys was popular among Twitter users, New Brand found.

Twitter is still figuring out how to extract the most value from its data business, which grew 53% over the past year, according to papers the company filed with the U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission. The company has clamped down on the amount of data that outside firms are allowed to pull from its system free of charge.

Many smaller analytics startups are now turning to four companies that Twitter has dubbed "certified data resellers." These brokers, Gnip, Data Sift, Topsy and the Japanese company NTT Data, account for the bulk of Twitter's data revenue. Last year, they paid Twitter monthly fees of about $35.6 million.

Twitter's exponential growth has meant its influence extends well beyond marketing and crisis public relations. Nonprofits, human-resource managers and politicians have found Twitter data useful, too. The Red Cross began analyzing Twitter data during 2012's Hurricane Sandy, says spokeswoman Anne Marie Borrego, using social-media-generated heat maps to direct aid. Twitter itself analyzed the site's chatter to predict the outcome of last year's U.S. presidential election.

But as Twitter plans to go public, some smaller firms worry that Twitter could further limit access to its valuable data. "Free," says Kristin Muhlner, CEO of New Brand Analytics, "isn't interesting to investors."

Investing in Funds & ETFs: A Quarterly Analysis --- The Experts: Insights From Our Online Panel

1,269 words
7 October 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
R13
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Last month's Investing in Funds & ETFs report raised issues for the full range of investors, from beginners seeking free information to those with more experience who may be considering whether so-called alternative investments, such as hedge funds, may be right for them. For answers, we turned to The Experts, an exclusive group of industry and thought leaders who engage in in-depth online discussions of topics covered in the report. Edited excerpts follow. To read more, go to WSJ.com/WealthReport.

How can people with limited assets and income get good investment advice?

First, Learn the Basics

Before you dive into implementing any advice, understand the basics (e.g., discount vs. full-service brokerages, ETFs vs. mutual funds). Next, assess your overall financial situation to see whether you are even in a position to invest. Do you have any debt to tackle first? And do you have an emergency savings account set up with enough cash to cover your living expenses for six-plus months (or more if you have a family)?

-- Alexa von Tobel, founder and CEO, LearnVest.com

Favorite Low-Cost Site

My favorite solution? Wealthfront. It's a Silicon Valley startup that offers incredibly well-constructed portfolios. It charges $0 for accounts less than $10,000 and 0.25% for accounts larger than that. For that, you get all-ETF portfolios based on the latest in modern portfolio theory, managed by Burton Malkiel (author of "A Random Walk Down Wall Street"). I am not in any way affiliated with Wealthfront.

-- Matt Hougan, president, ETF analytics, global head of editorial, IndexUniverse LLC

Four Sites for Free Advice

AAII has asset-allocation suggestions, T. Rowe Price has a great simulator for determining when and how to take Social Security benefits, Yahoo has a retirement budget tool, and Mint.com can help you track your spending so you might be able to save more.

-- Charles Rotblut, vice president with the American Association of Individual Investors

Free Tips From Bogleheads

Go to Bogleheads.org for advice. It's an online organization of knowledgeable investors who are ready to help anyone who asks. The site is operated by a nonprofit organization, so there is no commercialism, there are no hassles, and it's free. The site consists of the wiki and the forum. Both were built by volunteers dedicated to helping people begin or improve their investing journey through the application of sound investing principles. These values come from Vanguard founder Jack Bogle. I've been an active member of the community for more than a decade.

-- Rick Ferri, founder, Portfolio Solutions LLC, author and blogger

Go to Your Local Library

Start at the library and focus on savings and planning books first. There may also be charitable and governmental organizations that provide services for free, and some financial planners do pro bono work for those in need.

-- Tom Brakke, consultant, investment adviser

For investors who can't stomach much stock-market volatility, what do you recommend?

Balanced Is Best

Invest in a balanced fund. The combination of stocks and bonds in the fund should produce the portfolio return with lower volatility. At the same time, the investor would not be able to directly observe the impact of the equity component during market downturns, and would, therefore, be more likely to stay the course and less likely to overreact to the equity decline.

-- George U. "Gus" Sauter, senior consultant, former chief investment officer, Vanguard Group

Five Basic Tips

1. Don't talk about the stock market around the water cooler. Listening to others venting about their investments or giving financial advice will only stir up nervousness and doubt.

2. Get your advice and guidance from a financial planner. They are there to help you navigate the stock-market cycles.

3. Don't look at your portfolio daily.

4. Maintain your equity investments with a time horizon of at least seven years.

5. Continuously educate yourself by reading the newspaper finance section, but don't overdo it by having the business news on 24-7.

-- Michelle Perry Higgins, financial planner and principal, California Financial Advisors

Consider These Two Strategies

Rebalance your portfolio back to its intended allocation; use dollar-cost averaging, which is investing a set amount at regular intervals.

When rebalancing, you're forcing yourself to buy low and sell high. With dollar-cost averaging, you're buying more shares when prices are low and fewer shares when prices are high.

These habits can help investors discipline themselves to use market volatility to their advantage and resist the urge to do the worst thing, which is bail out and head for the hills.

-- Greg McBride, senior financial analyst and vice president, Bankrate.com

If It Makes You Unhappy, Stop

While it is important to try to make good decisions for the future, your investment approach should not make you miserable. Life is too short to chase returns if you are unhappy along the way.

-- Tom Brakke

Own Fewer Stocks

If your tolerance for financial risk is below your current equity allocation, then sell down to a level that's palatable -- and then stay there. This isn't a sign of weakness; it's a sign of intelligent decision making.

-- Rick Ferri

Do you recommend individuals hold "alternative" investments? And if so, which ones?

Not Appropriate for Most

Alternative funds, such as hedge funds, are more sexy than standard mutual funds. And, for some investors, they might provide some marginal advantage to their portfolio. However, I don't believe they are appropriate for most investors. While some alts have produced very impressive results, many have not.

Several years ago, when I was at Vanguard, a manager of a sovereign-wealth fund asked me how they could identify the best alt funds. My response was to look for the ones that are closed. Remember, you're not Yale or Harvard.

-- George U. "Gus" Sauter

Smart for Certain Portfolios

I believe that "alternative" investments can be a strong choice for certain portfolios. We have seen periods where alternatives have had a low correlation to traditional assets, thus lowering risk and increasing diversification. However, these investment choices are typically more complex, can have higher fees, offer less liquidity and may frustrate investors in good market times. Alternatives are not a one-size-fits-all solution. It would therefore take a thorough understanding of an investor's risk tolerance, long-term goals and objectives before making a recommendation.

-- Michelle Perry Higgins

Make Sure It Is Truly Alternative

Foreign-currency funds, gold and preferred stocks can all add alternative return characteristics for considerably less expense than most alternative funds being marketed heavily to advisers right now. Some might make sense, but I would take a hard look at their expenses and the length of their histories (many have been formed after the last bear market). I would use a wait-and-see approach to see how alternative they really are once the next bear market occurs.

-- Charles Rotblut

Higher Fees -- Not Higher Returns

No. Most are designed to extract higher fees from investors, not to deliver better returns.

-- Matt Hougan

No, Thank You

They tend to be complex, illiquid, nontransparent, hard to value and very expensive. Will investors be compensated enough to overcome the fees and lack of transparency and liquidity? Maybe, but I certainly would not invest my money in these products, so why would I ever recommend to my clients that they should?

-- George Papadopoulos, fee-only wealth manager

German fund buys Lisbon office block amid early signs of upturn

By Alice Ross in Frankfurt and Peter Wise in Lisbon 
511 words
6 October 2013
18:02
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2013 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

A German fund is to make the biggest property investment in Portugal since before the eurozone debt crisis, in the latest sign of a long-awaited rebound in the periphery's commercial real estate market.

Deka Immobilien will announce on Monday that it is paying  43m for the Bltico building, an office block in Lisbon, its first transaction in Portugal since the financial crisis five years ago.

The deal will be the largest acquisition by an international institutional investor in Portugal since 2009, according to data compiled by Real Capital Analytics, the commercial property group.

The acquisition of the 16-storey riverside building comes amid other early signs of an economic turnround in Portugal after three years of deep recession and record unemployment as the country struggles to comply with the terms of a  78bn international bailout.

Matthias Danne, head of Deka's real estate arm, said that the investor, which manages the largest open-ended property funds in Germany, was starting to dip a toe back into the waters of southern European property markets. The fund is also looking at opportunities in Spain and Italy.

"We think that the European Union will stick together: that is the most important question you have to ask yourself," he told the Financial Times.

"Lisbon is one of the most important cities in Europe and we are convinced that business will continue to happen there. Now is a good point in time to buy there as capitalisation rates [rates of return] and prices are reasonable even given the dangerous economic situation the country is in."

The deal is the latest sign that commercial property in peripheral eurozone states has become attractive to investors once more. The value of property transactions in Portugal, Italy, Ireland, Greece and Spain reached  2.3bn in the quarter to July, up 60 per cent from the first three months of the year, according to research for Cushman & Wakefield, a property consultancy.

Portugal, depressed by more than a decade of low economic growth, was passed over by the boom in property prices that swept through many other European countries from the mid-1990s.

According a recent report by Cushman & Wakeman, real estate investment has been one of the sectors hit hardest by the current crisis.

Falling demand for office space and difficult access to financing have damped investment. But Cushman & Wakeman say there is demand for "high-quality, well-located office buildings with long lease terms and a solid tenant base" of which there is a shortage in the Portuguese market.

The Bltico building was completed in 2010 in the Parque das Naes, a prime development area on the Tagus river in northeast Lisbon, an area that was transformed in the 1990s when it was chosen as the location for the Expo '98 World Exhibition. The building has five floors below ground and 16 above, including 13,400 sq metres of office space on 13 floors.
