Heard on the Street / Financial Analysis and Commentary

1,903 words
4 October 2013
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
32
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Winging It in the Shutdown

As far as anyone can tell, the U.S. job market is holding steady. But until the government shutdown ends, who really knows?

Wednesday, check-cutting giant Automatic Data Processing reported that private-sector employers added 166,000 jobs last month, a shade less than the 178,000 expected by economists. But the Conference Board reported its measure of online help-wanted ads reached its highest level on record. And on Tuesday, the Institute for Supply Management said its index of manufacturing employment rose to its highest level in more than a year.

Absent the shutdown, these would have been just a preamble to Friday's monthly employment report from the Labor Department, which economists were expecting to show a gain of 181,000 jobs. But with its publication on ice, investors, the Federal Reserve and businesses must rely on mostly private measures for a sense of how the job market is faring.

None of these are nearly as rich as the employment report. Take the ADP figures. Even putting aside the qualms many economists have over its methodology, it covers only jobs held at private-sector firms, while the so-called establishment survey used by the U.S. Labor Department includes government employers. The establishment-survey data also include important figures on how much workers are paid and how much time they put on the clock.

The jobs report also encompasses the entirely separate survey of households from which the unemployment rate, labor-force participation rate and other key measures are derived. Described by one prominent statistics textbook as a "massive and beautifully organized sample survey," no privately produced survey compares.

The data vacuum doesn't end with the jobs report. Key figures on international trade, inflation and retail sales will be postponed next week if the shutdown persists. So will the U.S. Department of Agriculture's monthly crop report, a crucial indicator not just for commodities traders but farmers and food companies.

Meanwhile, the Energy Information Administration says it has funds available to release its petroleum and natural-gas reports through next week. If the shutdown continues to the point where it can't provide reports, energy traders will lack some of the few reasonably reliable, and regular, data points in the global energy market. Heavy oil and gas users, such as chemical companies, would also have a tougher time gauging things.

Left by a prolonged shutdown to rely on a mishmash of private reports and available government data, trying to figure out what the economy is doing is "like trying to fly a huge airplane with just a few instruments," says economist Mark Zandi of Moody'sAnalytics, which prepares ADP's report. "You can fly it, but you can't fly it very well."

All of which would be bad enough without the threat of economic disruption from the shutdown and brewing fight over the debt ceiling. The plane could be flying into a storm.

-- Justin Lahart

Asian Superman Unloading Power

Hong Kong's "Superman" is running ahead of Ben Bernanke. Li Ka-shing, Asia's richest man according to Forbes, earned the moniker "Superman" in his Hong Kong hometown after decades of spotting the right trends. His empire today stretches from ports to grocery stores. He now is taking his Hong Kong utilities business public before the U.S. Federal Reserve starts winding down its bond purchases soon.

Mr. Li-controlled Power Assets Holdings, which runs electricity operations in Hong Kong, Europe and Australia, will spin off up to 49% of its Hong Kong unit. It may sell shares by the end of the year, though pricing and other details aren't out yet. The company says the listing is to gain "financial strength" so it can look for assets abroad.

Timing matters, though, and Mr. Li may be striking while the iron still is hot.

When interest rates are low, utilities stocks tend to do well, thanks to their steady profits and high dividends. But if the Fed pushes rates upward, valuations could cool look at what happened to U.S. utility stocks after May, when Fed chief Mr. Bernanke first hinted bond purchases might taper off soon. Hong Kong directly imports U.S. interest rates through its dollar peg.

Power Assets used to trade at 16.5 times forward earnings before Mr. Bernanke's whispers in late May. It is now at 14 times, but that is still rich compared with a 15-year average of 11.7, according to FactSet.

Mr. Li also may be getting out before regulations crimp profits. The return on assets that Hong Kong utilities are allowed are among the highest in the world, even though this city-state is a mature market like the West now. Regulators could reduce the rate to 7% or lower in 2018, from 10% today, CLSA says.

Mr. Li has a history of cashing out of richly valued businesses and deploying the cash into bargain bets. He exited from an Indian telecom business in 2007, listed a Hong Kong ports trust in 2011 and now is selling his Hong Kong supermarket chain. Investors hungry for yield drove up these businesses' valuations to some extent, but they all have questionable growth prospects.

This record should now make investors in Hong Kong utilities nervous. And which sector should feel encouraged? European infrastructure and telecoms, since that is where Mr. Li has plowed roughly $25 billion in the past four years.

-- Abheek Bhattacharya

Royal Mail

Carries

Heavy

Baggage

Royal Mail might look like a first-class play on one of the world's biggest e-commerce markets. But the landmark float of the U.K. postal operator, expected to be valued at up to <pound>3.3 billion ($5.35 billion) when it starts trading in London this month, comes with some heavy baggage.

A likely strike soon after Royal Mail's listing is a reminder of the company's Achilles' heel. The powerful Communication Workers Union should get the votes it needs to call a strike in protest over the privatization, pensions and pay. Sure, this would be the union's first nationwide strike since 2009, and any hit to Royal Mail's sales likely will prove temporary. But the re-emergence of labor-relations troubles is a concern.

The bigger challenge for Royal Mail is growth. There is no end in sight for a near decadelong slide in letter volumes, down 8% in the year to March. That makes sustaining growth in parcel volumes, which account for almost half of Royal Mail's sales, important if the group is to deliver targeted 2% to 3% annual sales growth over the next few years.

True, the U.K. spends more online per capita than any other market, which has boosted demand for delivery services. But just as letters are being replaced by email, the e-book market is growing rapidly while more shoppers are opting to collect online purchases from stores. Parcel volumes in the U.K. could expand 3% a year through 2018, down from an annual average 3.7% rise since 2008, estimates PricewaterhouseCoopers.

That could leave Royal Mail more reliant on price increases to sustain sales growth. But that isn't easy given there are some 15 logistics operators vying for a piece of Royal Mail's roughly 50% share of the U.K. parcel market by volumes. And cutting costs is getting harder now that Royal Mail is most of the way through a major restructuring. While operating-profit margins more than doubled to 4.4% last year, Royal Mail now is targeting a 0.5-percentage-point annual improvement.

Royal Mail's offering was fully subscribed within hours of its launch late last week. The 7% dividend yield at the midpoint of the price range certainly looks appealing. Even so, investors should hope the final price delivers a decent enough discount.

-- Renee Schultes

Ocado's Tricky

Overseas Delivery

Online grocer Ocado has proved itself to be a force in e-commerce. What is less clear is how much more it can deliver for investors.

The company, which sells fresh food online and trucks it to customers' doorsteps, had a number of earnings downgrades and a lousy stock performance in the first couple of years after its 2010 listing. But the stock has more than quadrupled this year and now trades at 32 times Ebitda. Part of that rally is justified. Ocado said in May it would manage an online delivery service for rival Wm. Morrison Supermarkets said in May it would manage an online delivery service for rival

. The deal included a hefty <pound>165 million ($267.7 million) cash payment that wiped out all of Ocado's debt.

But that is likely to be as good as it gets at least in the U.K., Ocado agreed to make Morrison its exclusive partner, ruling out more deals. And while analysts expect online grocery retailing to rise about 15% annually for the next few years, there is no guarantee Ocado will expand so fast. Larger rivals such as Tesco and J Sainsbury will be vying for their piece of the pie.

The real reason investors have gorged on Ocado is its prospects further afield. Ocado's edge lies in its sophisticated system that helps it reach wide areas with a small number of distribution centers. That could help another grocer that wants to jump-start its online operations quickly.

Yet in countries where people aren't already shopping for groceries online regularly, it would be hard to justify a large upfront payment to Ocado.

Other markets where online shopping is more advanced are very competitive. The U.S., for instance, will probably never be an option because domestic players such as FreshDirect have conquered the most attractive regions already. Amazon.com, the clear leader in online retail, delivers fresh food in Seattle and Los Angeles and reportedly has plans to reach dozens more with the help of its existing infrastructure. That leaves little room for a new player with no experience in the U.S. market.

Ocado looks set to thrive in the U.K. But investors waiting for international expansion may find the shares have spoiled before it makes the leap.

-- John Jannarone

Overheard

No one likes to stare into the abyss. But for U.S. banks, the experience is meant to be cathartic.

The biggest banks have again submitted to regulators so-called living wills, essentially guides to how to put them out of their misery in the event of a crisis. Such road maps should prove helpful in trying to understand the financial behemoths, which can include scores of legal subsidiaries in numerous countries around the world.

The exercise is meant to further insure against U.S. taxpayers again having to bail out failing financial giants.

But while helpful, it is far from a cure. For starters, much will depend in any future financial crisis on the willingness of regulators to break up a big bank.

And chances are that if one giant is teetering, a whole bunch are likely to be in trouble.

Even worse, taking down a big bank could spark a run on others.

Having a map is a start. But it doesn't guarantee you'll reach your destination.

-- overheard@wsj.com

LETTERS TO THE EDITOR
Ultimately, banks are ' big data ' and technology companies

255 words
4 October 2013
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
08
English
Copyright 2013 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

From Mr Zachary Townsend.

Sir, Banks are " Realising the promise of new technologies " (The Connected Business, FT.com, September 30), despite being saddled by outdated and outmoded core banking technologies. Some of the best but least studied innovation is happening inside treasury management and commercial banking groups. There, far from the public eye, new technologies - such as application programming interfaces - are allowing financial institutions and businesses of all sizes to interact in new, faster ways. These technologies speed the flow of transactions for the business while decreasing risk and delivery costs for the bank.

Unfortunately, Eric Openshaw and Larry Albin continue the false dichotomy between the business and technology functions of banks. Ultimately, banks are "big" data and technology companies. At the centre of every bank is simply a data store that records who has how much money and the rules governing transaction processing. We call this data store a core banking system, which is then surrounded by delivery channels, risk management and other technologies. Banks, their boards and their shareholders will come to realise that huge portions of bank budgets and headcounts can be replaced by modern web technology if architectured correctly. Those financial institutions that remake themselves fastest in the model of software engineering first companies such as Google or Facebook, including picking a forward-thinking technologist as chief executive, will be the true benefactors of this new era in finance.

Zachary Townsend,

Co-founder,

Standard Treasury,

San Francisco, CA, US

US budget politics: America goes dark

By Richard McGregor 
1,520 words
4 October 2013
19:12
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2013 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

It is no small job shutting down the US government.

On Tuesday morning, hours after the midnight deadline to pass a new budget, Washington bureaucrats went to work as usual, where they began issuing orders to colleagues to go home until further notice.

It took a day or two for the impact of the government shutdown to begin rippling across the country. Hundreds of thousands of workers were temporarily laid off; supermarkets on military bases were closed; medical research was halted and pantries for the poor were running out of food.

By week's end, the White House and its Republican foes were no closer to an agreement. Although fraying around the edges, conservatives in Congress were holding firm: they would not vote for a new short-term budget unless President Barack Obama's healthcare reforms, which became law in 2010, were delayed or gutted.

"The grand strategy can be summarised in two words: don't blink," says Chris Krueger of Guggenheim Partners, an asset manager.

These stand-offs have become almost routine since Tea Party Republicans swept into Congress in 2010. But the notion that this is just more of the same political kabuki ahead of an inevitable one-minute-to-midnight deal will vanish if Congress falls at its next hurdle - the October 17 deadline to allow the government to take on more debt to keep itself running.

The effects of America's political dysfunction in recent years have spilled over its borders, spreading the domestic economic damage around the world and rattling global stock and bond markets. But the failure to raise the so-called debt ceiling, leaving the US Treasury to scramble for funds to pay the country's debts, would dwarf the fallout from the last showdown in August 2011. That episode cost the US its triple A credit rating from Standard & Poor's.

"There is precedent for a government shutdown, but there is no precedent for a default," Lloyd Blankfein, the CEO of Goldman Sachs, said after emerging with fellow bankers from a White House meeting this week. "I'm not anxious to be part of the process that witnesses it."

If there is no deal, the US Treasury would run out of cash sometime between October 18 and November 5. With no legal ability to borrow, the US would have to immediately start cutting deeply into government programmes to prioritise payments among the 100m-odd it makes a month, to everyone from foreign creditors to pension recipients.

"It will be an instant crisis, complete bedlam" if the debt ceiling isn't raised, says Mark Zandi, an economist with Moody'sAnalytics.

The conventional wisdom of the moment has Mr Obama and the Democrats winning the politics of the shutdown, successfully painting the Republican leadership as captive to the Tea Party faction in the House of Representatives.

John Boehner, the Republican House speaker, has never forgotten how the last government shutdowns, in 1995 and 1996, worked out for his party. Newt Gingrich, then the speaker of the House, never recovered politically, and Bill Clinton coasted to a second term as president.

It is no surprise, then, that Mr Boehner opposed the idea of tying the new budget to striking a blow against Obamacare. Polls show that his instinct was right: regardless of how Americans feel about the Affordable Health Care Act, as it is officially known, they oppose the idea of holding the budget hostage in order to neuter it.

But Mr Boehner was cornered after a Tea Party rebellion last month, and he now cuts a beleaguered figure as he defends the position.

Mr Obama, pushed around by the Republicans in the 2011 budget clash, is piling on the pressure. "The only thing keeping the government shut down," he told a campaign-style rally near Washington on Thursday, "is that Speaker John Boehner won't even let the [budget] bill get a yes-or-no vote, because he doesn't want to anger the extremists in his party."

With the White House refusing to discuss changes to the heathcare reforms in budget talks, Mr Boehner's colleagues say he may be able to cut a deal - but only at the expense of his job.

The frustration at not being able to find a way out of the cul-de-sac that the Tea Party has pushed the leadership into is evident among the Speaker's colleagues in the hothouse of Capitol Hill. "We're not going to be disrespected," said Marlin Stutzman, a Republican congressman from Indiana, on Tuesday. "We have got to get something out of this, and I don't know what that even is." Mr Stutzman later apologised for his "careless" remarks.

Tea Party Republicans, far from being apologetic, insist they are exactly where they want to be. Tim Huelskamp, a Kansas Republican who led an unsuccessful attempt to topple Mr Boehner earlier this year, thinks he has already compromised by supporting a one-year delay in the core of Obamacare, rather than withholding funds from it altogether.

"You don't get the best choices up here [in Washington]," he said in an interview in his office this week, lamenting that the Republican leadership was not taking an even tougher line. "But this is about the budget. Obamacare is not going to save us money, Mr President!" When Mr Boehner's name comes up, he damns his leader with faint praise, saying: "He has moved quite a bit."

The White House also remembers the government shutdown wars of the mid-1990s, when Mr Clinton stared down Mr Gingrich. They are hoping that history will repeat itself.

But veterans of the Clinton White House caution about getting too cocky, saying victory was never preordained. "I remember day after day, we had significant debates about whether we were going to come out of this ahead or behind," says Mike McCurry, Mr Clinton's spokesman at the time.

The Republican leadership has tried to paper over the party's infighting this week, talking of compromise, and one by one, sent bills to the Senate funding various arms of the government without conditions. These stunts aside, however, the Republicans' best hope may be that, for all the clamour in Washington, much of the US heartland is still not tuning in.

Greater Washington, with its high concentration of federal workers, and areas with military bases or large national parks, have been immediately hit by the shutdown. In places such as the city of Normal, Illinois, however, the capital's crisis has yet to grip, except as a spectacle of political dysfunction.

"If this had been a year ago, when we were building this building, it would have been a nightmare," says Chris Koos, Normal's mayor, of its new municipal offices.

The adjoining cities of Bloomington and Normal are prosperous, largely white-collar communities surrounded by the broad plains of central Illinois. The biggest employers are two universities and the State Farm insurance firm. "This is as close to middle America as you can get," says Tari Renner, mayor of Bloomington.

The cities might lose some federal grants, and the low-income women lining up at the McLean County Health Department for extra food for their babies might soon have to look elsewhere. But Bloomington has few federal jobs to be cut.

The early signs of the crisis are there, however. Mr Koos says the city cancelled buying municipal bonds this week, despite the attractive rates on offer. "The market is too unpredictable right now," he says.

Politically, the same divisions tearing the Republicans apart in Washington are evident in Bloomington and Normal.

The country-club Republicans who once dominated the local party - Mr Boehner is the epitome of the breed - are being pushed aside by small-government conservatives. "The Republican leadership is just like the Democratic leadership: they just want to get re-elected," says Jerry Vogler, 66, who runs a service for veterans, funded by a county-level tax. "They like big government too much. They have gotten away from their roots."

Other local Republicans do not support their party's tough tactics in Washington. "I am not pleased about this kind of behaviour," says Mike O'Grady, who heads the local economic development council.

The split among local conservatives helped elect Mr Renner, who says he is Bloomington's first Democratic mayor since the city was founded in the 1860s.

Back in Washington, Mr Boehner has yet to blink. But he seems to be at least winking at the markets, with leaked stories saying he will make sure there is no default.

As speaker, Mr Boehner decides which bills will be brought to the House floor for a vote. The numbers are already there, among both Republicans and Democrats, to pass a budget and probably the debt ceiling.

But the Tea Party has made clear this will not happen without a fight.

"This is just the first play of (our commitment) to the 10-year path to balance the budget," says Mr Huelskamp. "You cannot do that with Obamacare on the books."

In the perpetual Wall Street rivalry between Goldman Sachs Group Inc. and Morgan Stanley, Morgan Stanley long claimed the lead in Internet and technology U.S. initial public stock offerings. This year, that is starting to change.

In landing the lead role on Twitter Inc.'s public stock offering, Goldman is pulling ahead of Morgan Stanley in the horse race for such deals for the first time in at least two years.

The win culminates Goldman's effort Goldman to rev up its Internet IPO franchise and strengthen its ties to startup founders and their backers. The firm launched a conference last year aimed at entrepreneurs, brought back a former star analyst to help lead its banking efforts in the industry and has made its top executives more visible in Silicon Valley.

A person familiar with Twitter's thinking said executives there also felt Goldman understands the company. The bank allows its traders and other employees to read tweets the company's 140-character messages to monitor news and rumors that could affect clients or move markets, people familiar with the matter said. Goldman also advertises on the social-media service. Those efforts helped the firm land the lead role on the IPO, the person familiar with Twitter's thinking said.

Internet deals, for the likes of Zynga, Pandora Media Inc. and Facebook Inc., aren't necessarily the biggest moneymakers on Wall Street. But what they lack in fee potential they often make up for in publicity and prestige, with the companies' flashy features and newly minted young millionaires in casual clothes.

And at a time when big banks are exiting from businesses, being cautious with their capital and tweaking algorithms to win slivers of market share in trading, the courting of technology companies pursuing stock offerings remains one of the most visible and fierce competitions.

IPOs are also an opportunity for banks to develop relationships with companies coming into the resources stock and cash for potential big acquisitions.

Goldman is no laggard in the business. Between them, Goldman and Morgan Stanley have been the first underwriting bank on nearly half of Internet and technology U.S.-listed IPOs since the beginning of 2011, according to Dealogic.

But Morgan Stanley had maintained a lead, winning the lion's share of lead assignments, in 32 of the 109 total deals since 2011.

In 2013, Goldman won nine lead Internet and technology assignments overall, more than Credit Suisse Group AG's six and Morgan Stanley's four, according to Dealogic. Goldman has also won more overall roles on technology and Internet IPOs, including nonlead roles, working on 18 deals this year, tied with Deutsche Bank AG, according to Dealogic. Morgan Stanley has been on 16 deals.

In part, the door for Goldman was opened by Morgan Stanley's role in Facebook's troubled IPO, according to people familiar with Twitter's thinking. Facebook's May 2012 IPO, the biggest-ever U.S. technology debut at $16 billion, was beset by a technology glitch on the Nasdaq Stock Market and also selling pressure from investors who thought the deal was too large and too richly priced. Facebook's share price has more recently risen well above its IPO price.

One of the people familiar with Twitter's thinking also said that while the company did consider what happened with Facebook, Morgan Stanley was very much in the running for the lead role, because of its still-formidable reputation and capabilities. According to Twitter's IPO filing, revealed publicly on Thursday, Morgan Stanley is the second bank on the deal after Goldman.

Morgan Stanley won the two biggest Internet IPOs so far this year as measured by the companies' market cap: online coupon-seller RetailMeNot Inc., and airplane Internet access provider Gogo Inc., according to Dealogic. Morgan Stanley has led the IPOs of eight of the last 10 Internet and software companies with a market capitalization over $1 billion at their IPOs.

Goldman snagged the biggest software deal of the year by market value, "big data" firm Tableau Software Inc., on which Morgan Stanley was the second underwriter. Last year, Goldman didn't have a role in Splunk Inc.

Working on some smaller IPOs also helped Goldman pull ahead, including Marketo Inc. and ChannelAdvisor Corp., which both raised less than $100 million each. A person familiar with those deals said that Morgan Stanley didn't pitch for them. Goldman also led the March $93 million IPO of Silver Spring Networks Inc., which had originally signed up Morgan Stanley as its lead banker in 2011, according to public filings.

One big prize is yet to be claimed: the IPO of Alibaba Group Holding Ltd., which is expected to raise more than $10 billion in a U.S. offering, people familiar with its thinking have said.

A key move on Goldman's part, according to people familiar with the bank's effort, included bringing back Anthony Noto as co-head of global technology, media and telecommunications banking. Mr. Noto is a former star dot-com company research analyst who had left in 2008 to work with the National Football League. He returned to Goldman as a banker in 2010.

Mr. Noto led the firm's efforts to win the Twitter assignment, while his co-head, George Lee, led the team that pursued the Facebook deal, people familiar with the matter said.

Goldman held a board meeting in the Bay Area in June, giving senior executives and directors access to regional clients. Senior executives, including President Gary Cohn, are frequent visitors, people familiar with the matter said.

COMPANIES
Monsanto snaps up Climate Corp; COMMODITIES

By Neil Munshi in Chicago 
417 words
3 October 2013
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed3
21
English
Copyright 2013 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Monsanto, the agricultural products company, is looking to take advantage of the data surrounding more extreme weather patterns with the acquisition of the Climate Corporation, a data science company that it expects to help raise crop yields.

The St Louis-based company said the cash acquisition, valued at roughly $930m, would give it leadership in the growing field of big data, giving farmers access to wide swaths of information.

Hugh Grant, chief executive, said data represented a potential $20bn industry-wide sales opportunity and linked the deal to climate change.

"Farmers today are challenged to make key decisions for their farms in the face of increasingly volatile weather conditions," Mr Grant told analysts.

"Because of this we believe there is a real opportunity and value in working with farmers to manage the risks that affect them every year."

Climate Corp's hyperlocal weather forecasting and risk management tools will bolster Monsanto's FieldScripts product, which provides farmers with field mapping, soil data, seed recommendations and other data points.

Farmers access FieldScripts via an iPad in their tractor cab which sets the planter on the basis of transmissions received from Monsanto. The product is being trialled by about 160 US farmers covering 40,000 acres.

Robert Fraley , chief technology officer, said the acquisition would help Monsanto's big data offerings grow "at a faster pace" internationally along with strengthening its core seed business.

Monsanto is not alone in capitalising on the demand for data to make agriculture more precise. Rival DuPont Pioneer launched its own subscription service, Field 360, this summer. It provides farmers access to soil, weather and precipitation information.

The news came as Monsanto reported a larger-than-expected loss during what has historically been its weakest quarter. It reported a net loss of $249m, or 47 cents a share, excluding one-time items, during the three months ending in August compared with $229m, or 42 cents a share, a year earlier.

Revenues rose 5 per cent to $2.2bn in the fiscal fourth quarter, compared with $2.1bn last year. Analysts had forecast a loss of 43 cents a share on $2.24bn in sales. The company said it would earn $5 to $5.20 a share in the fiscal year ending next August, including a 14 cent charge related to the Climate Corp deal.

The shares were down by 0.96 per cent, at $104.04, yesterday in New York.

It is nice to have something to distract investors from weak quarterly earnings. The bad news for Monsanto is that its diversion was a $900m acquisition of a data analytics start-up that is expected to cut 14 cents out of next year's earnings.

For the year, earnings did sprout 20 per cent, and the company predicts another 9 per cent to 13 per cent next year. But even that growth is not coming cheap. Excluding the just-announced acquisition of Climate Corp , capital expenditures will still leave free cash flow below the levels reached in recent years, an unusual pattern for a company that commands a premium valuation.

The global population keeps growing and prospering. That is good news for a company that offers corn and soyabean seeds along with herbicides to farmers. But the science involved is constantly advancing and there is plenty of competition from upstarts and large rivals such as DuPont and Syngenta. Monsanto spent $1.5bn last year on capital expenditures and research and development, a full fifth of sales. Free cash flow has been steady at about $2bn for the past three years. But with capital expenditures alone set to jump 50 per cent in 2014 to over $1bn, free cash flow, excluding the Climate acquisition, still drops to about $1.6bn.

Monsanto shares now trade at 11.5 times operating cash flow, exactly the midpoint of its valuation range since 2011. It has spent nearly all of free cash flow on returning capital to shareholders through buybacks and dividends. But its total shareholder return this year is just 13 per cent, below the overall market. And with an unspectacular 2014 forecast, Monsanto investors should not expect a bountiful harvest soon.

Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape

By Spencer Jakab 
417 words
3 October 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Fed Hawks

May Feast

On ISM

Maybe it wasn't so much a "close call" as a bad call.

A number of rate-setters at the Federal Reserve used words to that effect to describe their decision not to taper bond purchases at last month's meeting. The economic data just weren't quite strong enough. Some economists pointed to Wednesday's slightly disappointing figure on private-payroll growth as proof of their wisdom.

Evidence that the private economy isn't merely healthy, but positively humming, outweighs that, though. Exhibit A is one of the most useful and forward-looking data series out there. (And it is probably the best available given the government shutdown). The Institute for Supply Management's surveys of executives have an impressive record in signaling the beginnings and ends of economic expansions.

Earlier this week, the ISM Manufacturing Index registered a reading of 56.2 for September, the highest in 29 months. Because it has a longer track record, that index and its subcomponents often attract more attention. But the nonmanufacturing index, which is due Thursday, actually covers a larger swath of the U.S. economy -- nearly nine-tenths.

It has been even stronger lately, hitting 58.6 in August. That is the highest measure of service activity since December 2005. Economists polled by Dow Jones Newswires see the index dropping slightly to a still-stellar 57.

As a direct result of the decision not to curb bond buying, though, benchmark 10-year Treasury yields have slipped in a matter of weeks to just over 2.6% from nearly 3%. In normal times, such a sharp drop would come only in response to weak leading indicators.

Michael Shaoul, chief executive of Marketfield Asset Management, noted that "much of the U.S. economic data that has been released in recent weeks is utterly incompatible with the current level of yields offered in the bond market. Sooner or later, something will have to give, and we doubt that it will be the data which collapses."

There has been a 75% correlation between the level of the index and quarterly change in gross domestic product for the service and construction sectors, according to Haver Analytics. So, unless the ISM is giving false signals or the dysfunction in Washington turns into a full-blown fiscal crisis, the Fed will have a tough time justifying further foot dragging.

Corporate News: Monsanto to Buy Climate Corp. in Data-Science Push

By David Kesmodel 
746 words
3 October 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
B7
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Monsanto Co., the world's largest seed company by sales, agreed to buy farm-analytics firm Climate Corp. for about $930 million, deepening its push into technology designed to help farmers boost productivity and manage risk.

The purchase of closely held Climate Corp. will enable Monsanto to offer farmers a wider range of tools -- including individualized weather statistics -- as it seeks to build on its main business of selling genetically engineered seeds.

Monsanto is betting that tapping vast databases to help farmers increase their production will be one of the fastest-growing areas of agribusiness in coming years. It also expects weather and soil analytics to lead to significant gains in crop yields, complementing the biotechnology advances made by Monsanto and its rivals.

Such "precision farming" technology already has started to change how some farmers operate, and has attracted the interest of rivals including DuPont Co. Modern tractors can house tablet computers and GPS guidance systems that can automatically cover fields with seed or fertilizer tailored for local conditions, often without the vehicle's operator needing to steer.

Executives at St. Louis-based Monsanto, which has about $15 billion in annual revenue, said acquiring Climate Corp. would enhance the company's offerings to help farmers cope with threats like climate change and water shortages.

"We believe that data science has tremendous potential to boost productivity," said Kerry Preete, Monsanto's executive vice president of global strategy. In coming decades, "farmers around the world are going to be asked to produce more on their existing footprint of land."

San Francisco-based Climate Corp. was founded in 2006 by software engineers and data scientists who previously worked at Google Inc. and other Silicon Valley companies. The company's products include weather-monitoring and yield-forecasting data that farmers use to make decisions such as where and when to plant crops.

Farmers access the data through the company's Climate.com website or via mobile devices. The company also sells weather insurance and crop insurance through independent crop-insurance agents.

Climate Corp. Chief Executive David Friedberg told reporters that the company's clients include thousands of U.S. farmers, who account for several millions acres of crops. He declined to disclose the company's revenue.

Monsanto plans to integrate Climate Corp.'s weather information and analytics into a product it has been testing this year called FieldScripts, which farmers can access through an iPad in their tractor cabs. Using data on a farm's soil characteristics, the tool helps farmers maximize their yields by varying the amount of seed they apply in specific areas.

Monsanto has said FieldScripts could lead to an increase in corn yields of five to 10 bushels an acre, a gain of up to 7% based on average U.S. corn yields of about 148 bushels an acre over the past five years. The company plans to launch the product in four states next year.

Tim Tiberio, an analyst with Miller Tabak & Co., said the segment offers significant growth potential. But he said Monsanto also faces challenges, such as persuading large numbers of farmers to share data about their farms with the company.

Monsanto made a big move into the precision-farming arena with the purchase last year of Precision Planting Inc., a Tremont, Ill., company that has developed software tools to help farmers improve seed spacing and depth using a national database of soil conditions. That deal was valued at up to $250 million.

Separately, Monsanto reported a wider loss for its fiscal fourth quarter, hurt by lower seed sales, and forecast earnings for fiscal 2014 that trailed analysts' expectations.

For the quarter ended Aug. 31, Monsanto posted a loss of $249 million, or 47 cents a share, compared with a year-earlier loss of $229 million, or 42 cents a share. Excluding one-time items, the company had a year-earlier per-share loss of 44 cents.

Net sales for the quarter rose 5% to $2.2 billion. But sales at Monsanto's seeds and genomics business, its biggest contributor to revenue, fell 1.5% from a year earlier to $1.19 billion.

For the new fiscal year, Monsanto predicted per-share profit of between $5 and $5.20, below forecasts of $5.33 from analysts polled by Thomson Reuters.

Michael Hickins and Ben Fox Rubin contributed to this article.

Experian, the credit checking and information services company, has agreed to buy US-based fraud protection provider 41st Parameter in a $324m deal that boosts Experian's reach in fighting web and mobile fraud.

The cash purchase will help Experian expand its fraud prevention and identity management division to account for about a tenth of its overall business within the next few years, the company said on Tuesday.

"[41st Parameter ] really fills a hole [in technology] that we would not be able to develop in real time to get to market as fast as we can by acquiring," said Peg Smith , executive vice-president of investor relations at Experian.

Ms Smith said Experian was drawn to 41st Parameter 's technology, which can identity devices that people use to access an online bank account, for example, to determine whether that device would put the transaction at risk.

She said their device detection capabilities added "another layer" in fraud prevention, and would bolster Experian's existing data analytics system.

Experian's fraud prevention operations already generate about 6-7 per cent, or $300m, of its overall revenue. The company said a proliferation of mobile devices and the growth in ecommerce would fuel an increasing demand for products and services that prevent web fraud.

41st Parameter 's clients range from banks and travel companies to payment service providers, and Ms Smith said Experian would be able to sell the technology to its clients worldwide.

"This adds pretty material scale to the US [business] but we're really looking at it as a global capability," she said.

She added that 41st Parameter had been limited in its ability to grow because of the size of their sales force.

Analysts at Shore Capital said: "We assume that [the acquisition] brings to Experian comparative advantage that will enhance segment growth and bring capability that differentiates Experian, further strengthening its position in a market segment that undoubtedly has a clear view of growth."

Experian, which is also widely known for its credit ratings business, said it was expecting 41st Parameter to generate about $26m of revenue for the year ending December 31 2013. They added that the acquisition was expected to be neutral in terms of earnings per share in the year ending 31 March 2014.

41st Parameter 's investors include Norwest Venture Partners, a venture capital firm that has invested in ecommerce start-ups, and Jafco Ventures , a California-based firm that invests in communications and software companies.

Experian's share price closed up slightly at 11.87p on Tuesday, a gain of 0.85 per cent.

FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT: IGNITES RETAIL FUNDS / ALTERNATIVES
Retirement plans put new dishes on the menu; Pensions

By Emile Hallez 
874 words
30 September 2013
Financial Times
FTFTA
Surveys FIG1
04
English
Copyright 2013 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Funds are looking at diversifying to counter volatility, writes Emile Hallez

Alternative assets and investment strategies are gradually gaining ground in retirement plans, as mutual fund companies address -volatility by diversifying holdings in target-date funds and other products.

Few of the standard options on the US 401(k) pension plan menus emphasise alternatives, but target-date funds - which increasingly hold alternatives as underlying investments - are growing faster than any other product type.

Defined contribution (DC) plan options are becoming simpler, as plan sponsors reduce the number of investments available to participants and often default them into target-date funds.

"Plan sponsors are understanding that they need to limit the number of choices. If you have too many choices, people get immobilised," says Fred Barstein, founder of The Retirement Advisor University, noting that outside of plans with more than $1bn in assets, sponsors hesitate to put alternative funds on plan menus.

Treasury Inflation Protected Securities (Tips), real estate investment trusts (Reits) and commodities accounted for 7 per cent of the assets in target-date funds, according to data for 2012 provided by Target Date Analytics.

But the small holdings of individual asset classes mean each amount is insufficient to make any real difference to portfolio diversification, one expert notes.

"If you can achieve full meaningful diversification without adding emerging markets bonds, then adding emerging markets bonds is simply not a big factor in the overall success of that portfolio," says Joe Nagengast, principal of Target Date Analytics.

"The focus on alternative asset classes is usually misplaced. What we should be focusing on is the overall effective diversification of the portfolio - not the latest hot buzz asset class."

Target-date fund providers have avoided dedicating a larger portion of asset allocation to alternatives for several reasons, among them liquidity constraints and that fact that alternatives can be more difficult for 401(k) participants to understand than traditional asset classes such as equity and fixed income.

Though target-date funds are the most common product through which 401(k) investors have exposure to alternatives, some DC plans have also added real estate or Tips funds to their investment options. Those funds are typically diversified, composed of holdings in other mutual funds.

"They're not necessarily getting pure commodities as one fund - they're getting it as a piece of a fund that's invested in things like Tips and Reits," says James Veneruso, a vice-president at research company Callan Associates. About one third of DC plans offer such investment options, Mr Veneruso says, noting that the recent proliferation of such mutual funds has been fuelled by the decline in defined benefit (DB) plans.

As asset management companies see business in DB plans taper, some are weighing options in the retail market. KKR and Blackstone have recently established mutual funds.

Eighty to 90 per cent of individual retirement account rollover assets come from DC plans, a detail well known to private equity and hedge fund firms, Mr Barstein says.

"They're really eyeing this market," he says. "This is a tremendous distribution vehicle for them."

Since Callan created its DC index in 2006, DB plans have outperformed DC plans by an average of 1.2 per cent annually. The difference is largely due to the use of alternatives and hedge-fund like strategies in DB plans, Mr Veneruso says.

Mr Barstein says, given the potential to increase long-term performance, alternatives will inevitably see greater allocations within target-date funds, and therefore 401(k) plans.

"It's not a question of whether there are going to be alternatives. It's just when, and in what form," he says.

"It's all going to come to asset allocation, because that is where people are investing anyway."

In a survey of target-date managers conducted last year, Callan found that 3 per cent of such target-date funds invested in private real estate, 43 per cent had allocations to global Reits, 47 per cent used commodities, while 20 per cent -indicated that they invested with an alternative strategy such as absolute return.

To address liquidity concerns, fund managers have added cash buffers and have offered real estate only as a portion of a fund.

Multiple Reits are often bundled within the same mutual fund, Mr Veneruso explains. Fund companies that emphasise active management are more likely than index fund providers to offer alternative strategies within retirement plans, he says.

The ranks of target-date providers that have incorporated alternatives include Putnam Investments, Invesco, Prudential and Pimco.

Putnam's funds include holdings in the company's line of Dynamic Asset Allocation and absolute return funds.

Prudential, which is in the midst of rebranding its four-year-old target-date series, incorporates a higher percentage of non-traditional assets as a fund progresses along its glide path, when an investor's assets have less time to recover from volatility.

"We think it helps produce better outcomes through having the diversity," says Joan Bozek, -senior vice-president of investment products at -Prudential. "It has not had a huge impact. But it has had an impact in maintaining competitive, reliable returns overall."

Business
Digital Diligence, Doubts Show at Ad Gathering; Marketers Prove Willing to Experiment but Aren't Convinced of Results

By Suzanne Vranica

The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

At this year's Advertising Week gathering in New York, it was clear that marketers were willing to try just about any digital experiment from syncing TV ads with tablet spots, to embedding ads in Twitter streams, to letting machines automate ad buying.

But, despite all the excitement, marketers still aren't sure if all their digital initiatives are helping them reach new audiences. A survey released this week by Adobe Systems Inc. showed that just 9% of marketers strongly agreed with the statement "I know our digital marketing is working."

Beyond serving up more than 100 seminars on topics like "Big Data and the Death of the Big Idea," the annual marketing jamboree, which began Monday, gave executives a chance to schmooze and swap notes on the sweeping changes in the ad industry. Cocktail-hour gossip ranged from the implications of the pending merger between industry giants Omnicom Group Inc. and Publicis Groupe SA to Twitter Inc.'s initial-public-offering plans.

Madison Avenue's elite have been cozying up more and more to their Silicon Valley counterparts. Facebook Inc. threw a party on the High Line, an elevated park in Manhattan, at which Keith Weed, Unilever's chief marketing officer, chatted with Sheryl Sandberg, Facebook's chief operating officer, partygoers said.

Chuck Porter, chairman of ad agency Crispin Porter + Bogusky, was seen at a poker game hosted by online-game maker Zynga Inc., sporting a velvet smoking jacket and munching sliders.

"There is a genuine relationship aspect to this business," said Tim Spengler, chief executive officer of Magna Global, a media-buying arm of Interpublic Group of Cos.

The Adobe survey found that 76% of marketers think marketing has changed more in the past two years than in the past 50.

At one discussion, called "Creative Carousel," top ad executives spoke of the mounting demands on ad creators as technology offers new advertising opportunities. Agency talent needs to know as much about Facebook posts and sponsored tweets as traditional formats, they said.

"Sometimes I miss the days when I had a print ad and a radio spot," said Carlos Figueiredo, a creative director at PublicisKaplan Thaler. He said it feels like there are "a billion" ad options.

On the digital front, a priority for advertisers is figuring out how to amplify their "first screen," or television, ads with spots on other screens  whether laptops, smartphones or tablets.

Twitter is proving a popular outlet for making that happen. The social-media service has been signing up media partners and advertisers for a program that puts ad-supported video clips into users' feeds. The latest example: the National Football League said this week that it will put football- game clips on Twitter that will include short ads from sponsors including Verizon Wireless.

Such Twitter deals still represent a tiny share of spending. The sponsorships for the NFL deal, for example, are in the low-eight-figure range for the 17-week regular season and playoffs, according to a person familiar with the matter. By contrast, the price of a single 30-second spot on "American Idol" last year ranged from roughly $300,000 to $500,000, according to ad buyers.

The music app Shazam offers another way to link TV with laptops and mobile devices. The app's audio fingerprint-recognition technology started out as a way to identify a song playing in a bar or on a car radio. But marketers are putting it to use to augment TV advertising.

When viewers watch certain TV ads and want more information, they can hold Shazam up to the set and it will transfer the information to their phones. Y&R, an ad firm owned by WPP PLC, talked up its collaborations with tech companies like Shazam at one of the panels.

Meanwhile, Vivaki, the digital arm of Publicis Groupe, touted Zeebox, a service that allows marketers to place ads on mobile devices that are synced up with ads running on TV.

Some in the ad industry say this is overkill, and potentially distracting to consumers. But marketers are trying it out. Vivaki ran campaigns for brands such as Kraft Foods Group Inc.'s Mio flavored water drops. Ads running on the Zeebox app saw click-through rates of about 39%, said Beth Doyle, vice president of Innovations at Vivaki.

Based on the numbers, marketers are embracing the digital chaos. Digital-ad spending will represent about a quarter of world-wide ad spending by 2014, up from 20.7% last year, according to a report released Wednesday by eMarketer and Publicis's Starcom MediaVest Group.

That isn't to say the focus at this year's gathering was all digital. The traditional media businesses went all out. Clear Channel Media & Entertainment, a unit of CC Media Holdings Inc., wined and dined marketers such as Bank of America Corp. with the help of mini egg rolls, cookies and milk and a serenade from singer John Mayer.

"It's at the highest level its been this year. Over the past few days, it has climbed significantly - from 61m to about 67m."

US indices were volatile for much of the day, opening lower, rising in midday trading, then falling sharply after reports came out that Walmart had cut back on new orders due to rising inventories. The claims, which Walmart denied, helped send shares down 1.5 per cent to $74.65.

The S&P 500 closed off 0.3 per cent to 1,692.77. The financial sector was the strongest area, up 0.5 per cent, while the healthcare sector declined 0.8 per cent.

The S&P remains 1.6 per cent off its record highs of last week. The same cannot be said for the Russell 2000 index of small-capitalisation companis, which is only 0.3 per cent off its all-time high.

The Russell 2000, which tracks smaller companies with a median market capitalisation of $616m, briefly touched an intraday record of 1,082, but ended the day off 0.1 per cent to 1,073.51.

JPMorgan Chase supported the buying as investors learned that the bank was in talks to settle allegations over wrongdoing in the mortgage securities market for about $4bn.

Shares of the bank were up 2.7 per cent to $51.70.

Cruise ship operator Carnival extended Tuesday's fall with another decline, as analysts from JPMorgan, Nomura, and Credit Suisse downgraded the company after it announced disappointing third-quarter results.

Carnival shares lost 5.3 per cent on the day to $32.70, and are down 12.6 per cent in the past two days.

Car parts retailer AutoZone added 2.6 per cent to $425.07 despite reporting fourth-quarter results that missed expectations. The company has been boosted in 2013 by continued strength from US car manufacturers, and is up 19.3 per cent on the year to date.

The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 0.4 per cent to 15,273.26, and the Nasdaq Composite Index finished down 0.2 per cent to 3,761.10.

The day's decline was a fifth-consecutive negative session for the S&P and the Dow.

While there is concern that a possible government shutdown and a debt ceiling debate may spook investors leading into Halloween, the recent selling has been far from a rush for the exits.

"You're seeing the market cautious, but you're not seeing panic in the market," said Quincy Krosby, market strategist at Prudential Financial.

Political wrangling in Washington continues to loom over the market, she added, but investors have seen a government shutdown before, along with its effect on the markets.

Aviva division head leaves after year in job

By Alistair Gray, Insurance Correspondent 
573 words
24 September 2013
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCMA
English
Copyright 2013 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

The head of one of Aviva's biggest divisions is leaving the FTSE 100 insurer after little more than a year in the job in the latest management shake-up under Mark Wilson, its new chief executive.

Robin Spencer joined one of Aviva's predecessor companies almost 20 years ago but has run the UK & Ireland general insurance business only since July last year.

He will be replaced by Maurice Tulloch, who runs Aviva's Canada business, after Mr Wilson said he wanted to have "the right people in the right jobs".

Insiders said that while the new chief executive regarded the financial performance of the UK general insurance operation as satisfactory, he wanted a new face to lead efforts to expand the business.

They said Mr Wilson feels that the division, which sells products including motor, home and commercial insurance, could do more to capitalise on Aviva's extensive life business, possibly through more cross-selling.

Aviva rebuffed a 5bn offer for the UK general insurance business from rival RSA three years ago during the tenure of Andrew Moss, former chief executive who quit after a pay row last year.

Some analysts think Aviva's recent rejig of its complex financial structure - the holding company owes billions of pounds worth of internal debt to the division - would make it easier to sell the operation.

However, senior Aviva managers have denied this is the rationale for the changes to the group's capital structure.

The UK general insurance business is among the most cash generative of Aviva's units.

However, it operates in one of the most competitive markets for insurance in the developed world and Mr Spencer - who is also a former chief executive of Aviva Canada - has led a cost-cutting push.

For every pound in premiums it received last year, it incurred 98p in claims costs and other expenses. This was the same as the year before.

In contrast, the general insurance business in Canada incurred 93p in claims costs and other expenses last year - an improvement from 95p a year earlier.

Mr Tulloch delivered a 63 per cent rise in profits at the Canada business during his tenure as chief executive, since 2009.

He has sought to expand Aviva's use of "predicative analytics" to price insurance policies more accurately - modelling, for example, how the height of buildings affects wind speeds and the risks of storm-related damage to nearby property.

Mr Wilson has said Aviva should be better at sharing expertise across its internal operations and Mr Tulloch's arrival in the UK should herald the expansion of such techniques in the insurer's domestic markets.

He joined one of the insurer's predecessor companies in 1992 and has spent most of his career in Canada - home to Aviva's second largest general insurance business after the UK - apart from a two-year spell in London.

In a statement, Mr Wilson said Mr Tulloch had an "impeccable track record" and was "the best leader to take the UK business to the next stage of its development."

He added: "Robin has done a fine job for the group, most recently leading the UK & Ireland GI business through the transformation and expense savings programme.

I would like to thank Robin for his considerable contribution to Aviva and he leaves the group with our best wishes."

Tech
FireEye, Rocket Fuel Surge as Investors Boost Tech IPOs

Danny Yadron 
812 words
24 September 2013
17:42
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Shares of Internet security firm FireEye Inc. soared on their first day of trading Friday, benefiting from investor interest in the security sector following some high-profile hacks at companies and government agencies in recent years.

The successful initial public offering comes even as some question whether the market can support so many antihacking companies, many of which rely on similar technology.

Meanwhile, another hot IPO by Rocket Fuel Inc. suggests that investors also are optimistic about some advertising-technology companies.

Both companies' shares more than doubled in early trading Friday, though they gave back some of those gains later in the trading session.

FireEye, a Silicon Valley company led by veteran software executive Dave DeWalt, priced its first round of shares Thursday at $20, raising $280 million. The stock closed at $36, up 80%, giving it a market capitalization of about $4.2 billion.

The valuation might seem high for a company with $83.3 million in revenues in 2012. Mr. DeWalt said the "evil emanating from the Internet" is creating a surge in spending for companies like FireEye. "There's going to be a tectonic shift in spending and someone is going to get that," he said. "We have as good a chance as anybody."

Mr. DeWalt's past exploits include selling McAfee, a bigger security company known for antivirus software, to Intel Corp. for $7.68 billion. Last year he joined FireEye, which was founded in 2004. He now holds stock worth nearly $166 million at Friday's closing price.

FireEye seeks to block the most advanced hackers by observing the behavior of their code, rather than the longtime approach of matching incoming software with known malicious programs. It does this by testing code downloaded onto a computer in a sort of digital petri dish walled off from the rest of the network.

In theory, this allows the company to block dangerous code never seen before called a "Zero-Day" attack simply because it acts like something up to no good.

However, many larger companies, including Trend Micro Inc. of Japan, Palo Alto Networks Inc. and AhnLab Inc. of South Korea use similar approaches and are improving their products, some security experts say.

"It's a bit of a bubble," said Adam Hils, a researcher on cybersecurity technology at Gartner Inc., of the buzz around FireEye. "If they're going to be a viable product company, they need to broaden their portfolio and they're doing that."

"There is a limit to what the market can absorb," said investor Ted Schlein, whose firm Kleiner Perkins Caufield & Byers has invested in many security companies.

FireEye has already expanded its offerings, and they work on servers and data centers instead of just individual machines, according to Mr. DeWalt.

For the six months ended June 2013, FireEye reported a $67.2 million loss. Its revenue for the same period more than doubled to $61.6 million.

Like other companies that offer software as a subscription service, FireEye books much of its revenue over time rather than as it cuts sales deals.

Shares of advertising firm Rocket Fuel, meanwhile, shot up 93% to close at $56.10 in its first trading session Friday. The company fared much better than some other advertising-related IPOs of the recent past.

Most ad technology companies operate networks in which advertisers and publishers talk to each other about when and where to place ads. Rocket Fuel, founded in 2008, instead primarily serves advertisers with an artificial-intelligence program that makes rapid decisions about when and where to place ads.

George John, Rocket Fuel's chief executive and co-founder, said that talking about Rocket Fuel's big data use "really helped" with general tech investors on the roadshow. "A lot of people were talking about big data, and a lot of them wanted to know more about the [artificial intelligence] business on top of it," he said.

As for why investors seem more enthusiastic about advertising startups, factors include more mature companies going public and a greater familiarity with online, or cloud, offerings in a variety of sectors, said Nelson Griggs, senior vice president at Nasdaq OMX Group Inc., where FireEye and Rocket Fuel listed on Friday.

"The kind of technology [IPOs] getting done have shifted from the pure cloud companies to companies doing things important to the cloud," Mr. Griggs said.

Corrections & Amplifications Rocket Fuel Inc.'s stock rose 93% in its initial trading session Friday. An earlier version of this article incorrectly reported the increase as 83%. Also, Adam Hils is a researcher on cybersecurity technology at Gartner Inc. An earlier version of this article incorrectly referred to him as Adam Hills.

Corporate News -- The Week Ahead: Digital Marketing Takes Center Stage

By Suzanne Vranica 
790 words
23 September 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
B2
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

At first glance, Kardashian mother Kris Jenner, the E*Trade baby, singer-songwriter John Mayer and Twitter seem to have little in common. Then again, the Advertising Week in New York conference tends to create strange bedfellows.

Roughly 90,000 advertising and marketing professionals are expected to participate in the 10th annual Advertising Week in New York, which begins Monday. The weeklong event, which includes roughly 250 seminars and parties, will seek to show off how well Madison Avenue has embraced digital marketing, such as social media to mobile advertising.

Digital advertising continues to be a bright spot in the ad industry and it is expected to grow nearly 14% in the U.S. to $42.26 billion this year, according to research firm eMarketer.

Companies such as Facebook Inc. and Amazon.com Inc., along with more than 100 advertising technology startups, are expected to show off their latest products this week and attempt to woo dollars from ad firms and marketers, such as PepsiCo Inc., Macy's Inc. and Priceline.com Inc.

A focus of the event will be the rise of "big data" -- the use of powerful computing to analyze troves of information.

The push signifies how the marketing business is becoming "equal parts art and science," said Matt Freeman, an operating partner at Bain Capital and chairman of Advertising Week. More data are underpinning things such as media buying, product development and creative development, he said.

Madison Avenue is becoming increasingly reliant on data and analytics. Advertisers are using information on consumers' purchasing histories and their online behavior to help guide companies' ad-buying decisions.

What people say on social media sites such as Twitter is being incorporated into the ad-creation process, ad executives said.

When Publicis Groupe SA and Omnicom Group Inc. announced their pending $35 billion merger this summer, the companies played up that boosting data analytics was part of the logic of the deal.

On Tuesday, RadiumOne Inc., an online-ad firm that targets consumers based on what they share on social media, will host a panel about how marketers are "applying and finding golden nuggets in the data that helps them solve business problems," said Eric Bader, RadiumOne's chief marketing officer.

Meanwhile, Acxiom Corp. is expected to debut a new product that will allow marketers and ad firms to use more data in the media-buying and planning process in online and offline media.

Data also will be a theme Monday, when AOL Inc. hosts the first of the "upfront" presentations for advertisers for its automated ad-buying businesses. With its upfront presentation, AOL is taking a page from the television business, which holds an annual event where marketers commit to buying TV ad time in advance of the new fall season.

AOL is hoping to persuade advertisers to earmark more of their ad dollars for "programmatic ad buying," an increasingly crowded sector in which ads are bought through automated exchanges, often in real time.

Programmatic buying will account for a small portion of the estimated $505 billion that marketers world-wide are expected to shell out this year, but it is growing fast.

Real-time bidding for digital display ads in the U.S. is expected to jump 73.9% this year to $3.34 billion, according to eMarketer.

For the nongeek crowd, Ms. Jenner will talk about the Kardashian brand, while Mr. Mayer will serenade deep-pocketed marketers on behalf of media consulting firm MediaLink LLC and Clear Channel Media & Entertainment, a unit of CC Media Holdings Inc.

Ad characters such as such as StarKist Co.'s Charlie the Tuna, Travelocity.Com's Roaming Gnome, and Toucan Sam, who hawks Kellogg Co.'s Froot Loops, will ride on a double-decker bus through Times Square as they compete to be inducted into Advertising Week's Madison Avenue Walk of Fame.

U.S. News --- THE OUTLOOK: Housing's Pillars Get Rethink

By Nick Timiraos 
883 words
23 September 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Washington is finally talking seriously about how to replace Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac, the mortgage-finance juggernauts that the government was forced to rescue five years ago. Just don't expect quick action.

The firms are proving to be as difficult to shut down as the U.S.-operated Guantanamo Bay prison in Cuba. Republicans and Democrats are deeply divided over what to do. On the surface, the disagreements concern what role the government should play in the mortgage market.

But the real debate boils down to this: Should all Americans continue to have relatively easy access to the pre-payable, 30-year, fixed-rate mortgage?

American homeowners love the 30-year mortgage, which isn't available in most other countries. It provides payments that are stable for the life of the loan, which makes finances easier to manage. In many other countries, homes are financed with adjustable-rate mortgages, where payments rise and fall with prevailing interest rates.

The government plays an unusually large role in the U.S. mortgage market because banks don't like holding 30-year mortgages. During the 1980s, many savings-and-loan associations failed when rates jumped because the interest they had to pay to depositors soared above the payments they received on those 30-year mortgages. This is known as "interest-rate risk."

Enter Fannie and Freddie. They don't make loans. Instead, they buy them from lenders, package them into securities, and sell those to investors. They promise to make investors whole when mortgages default. In other words, they take the credit risk.

This middleman role is important. It matches banks with pension funds and other investors that are more willing to take on the interest-rate risk. The companies also set uniform standards that facilitated very liquid securities markets.

The 30-year mortgage is available mainly because of government guarantees -- implied before the crisis and explicit since 2008.

Those who want the government out of the mortgage business say the 30-year fixed isn't all it's cracked up to be. Because borrowers pay a lot of interest during the first few years of the loan, it's hard to quickly build equity. This makes the loan less practical in an era where people move, switch jobs, and get divorced more often than in the past.

Defenders, however, say it's the wrong time to push more people into adjustable-rate loans because interest rates are likely to rise over the coming decade.

Wouldn't banks still offer the 30-year fixed mortgage without a government guarantee if it's so popular? Maybe, but they would likely require bigger down payments and higher rates.

It's a math issue. There is nearly $10 trillion in mortgage debt outstanding today with around $4.5 trillion backed by Fannie and Freddie. Scrapping the government's role means finding trillions of dollars ready to absorb the credit and interest-rate risk for new mortgages, since the firms have backed around two-thirds of those made since 2009.

Of course, every overhaul plan calls for more private capital. That goal faces three challenges:

First, private capital had a lousy track record during the bubble. Loans securitized without government backing performed worse than Fannie's or Freddie's, largely because investors relied too heavily on triple-A ratings. Between 2006 and 2012, Fannie, Freddie, and federal agencies suffered $206 billion in mortgage losses, or around 3.9% of all loans they guaranteed, according to data from Moody'sAnalytics. Privately issued securities, by contrast, faced $449 billion in losses, or 20% of all loans.

Second, a mostly private market could further concentrate lending in the largest banks -- exacerbating the "too big to fail" problem. In 2008, the top five banks in Canada, the U.K. and Australia, for example, funded more than half of all mortgages. During the crisis, many European banks were viewed as implicitly guaranteed by the government. In the U.S., pushing more mortgages into federally insured banks also doesn't get the government off the hook, a lesson already learned during the S&L crises of the 1980s.

Third, private capital takes on more risk in good times but exits quickly at the first sign of trouble. This tends to magnify the boom-and-bust nature of real estate. Downturns are worse because credit is hardest to find when it's most needed.

Several overhaul proposals propose creating federal reinsurance for mortgage-backed securities. Any government guarantee would be explicit and paid for by investors, akin to deposit insurance provided by the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp.

Government insurance solves some problems while creating others. Chiefly, taxpayers could face losses if capital levels or fees are set too low for this new insurance.

Fannie and Freddie, which held far too little capital to withstand the downturn, had for years argued successfully that higher capital standards translated to more costly mortgages, meaning less homeownership. Today, some worry that the housing industry would eventually make similar arguments for lower fees.

No one said an overhaul would be easy. Ultimately, Washington will have to be honest about what it wants and what that will cost.

FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT
F&C Investment Trust suffers from being too small in Japan; Fund focus

By Eleanor Lawrie 
575 words
23 September 2013
Financial Times
FTFTA
Surveys FNM1
13
English
Copyright 2013 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Manager Jeremy Tigue is unsure if emerging markets offer value yet, says Eleanor Lawrie

F&C Investments' Jeremy Tigue, who runs the 2.7bn Foreign & Colonial Investment Trust, has admitted he missed out on the recent surge in Japanese stocks, calling it his "biggest mistake" of the year.

Japan has left other markets standing since last November, when Prime Minister Shinzo Abe began a successful general election campaign with the mandate of introducing a number of monetary-easing measures to devalue the yen and fuel economic growth.

From November 11 2012 to mid-September this year the Nikkei 225 index of leading Japanese shares rallied by roughly 60 per cent, compared with a 25 per cent rise in the MSCI World index of global shares.

But Mr Tigue had a 4.7 per cent weighting to Japan, an underweight position when compared with the 7.2 per cent of the trust's benchmark FTSE All World index, which the trust adopted as its benchmark at the start of 2013.

"I put my hands up and admit I got [Japan] completely wrong," he says.

"That's been my biggest mistake this year."

The manager has delivered returns of 16.5 per cent in the year to date, in line with his peers within the AIC Global Growth sector, according to FE Analytics.

Away from Japan, the manager says he has begun to brace his investment trust for a fall in markets and has removed some of the borrowing, which has contributed to the year-to-date performance.

Mr Tigue says the level of gearing, a form of borrowing that amplifies investment gains or losses, had been reduced to 12 per cent and would fall further to 10 per cent. The trust's board allows a maximum of 20 per cent - a ceiling the manager neared in 2011.

"We are definitely more cautious than we were a year ago, and in September 2011," he says.

"The eurozone was going down the tube [at that time] and US debt had been downgraded, which was a huge deal. But we thought that was ridiculous. Companies were still making a profit so we were pretty bullish and increased our gearing," he says.

He adds: "The opportunities that were there a year ago are not there now."

Mr Tigue's view extends to emerging markets. The manager says he is unsure if valuations have fallen enough to present buying opportunities. Stock prices are still "not that cheap", according to Mr Tigue.

The manager has 8.5 per cent of his portfolio exposed to emerging market stocks, compared with 15 per cent at the end of 2010.

Elsewhere, the manager says his fund would benefit from the expiry in December 2014 of a costly debenture, a 110m loan the trust took out at an interest rate of 11.25 per cent.

Mr Tigue says he and the board were "wrestling" with what to do with the money that will be at the trust's disposal once the debt instrument expires and interest payments stop. A note from Winterflood Securities, the market maker, says the debenture's market value would fall to par in December 2014, adding 15m or 2.5p per share to the net value of the trust's assets.

Eleanor Lawrie is a reporter at Investment Adviser
